What to Do When the Wiping Period Ends
States ruling and disputes with Shafi'i and others

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when the wiping period ends and distinguish it from the Shafi'i and other positions.

Core Idea
When the wiping period ends, only the feet need washing—the rest of wudu remains intact.

In this lesson, we examine the ruling of what to do when the period for wiping over khuffs expires. The Hanafi school holds a moderate position: unlike al-Shafi'i, who requires a full wudu, and unlike Ibn Abi Layla or Hasan al-Basri, who require nothing, the Hanafi school requires only washing the feet. This balanced view is based on a nuanced understanding of how hadath affects the limbs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal scenario
The source text deals with the case when the prescribed period for wiping over khuffs comes to an end. The person was in a state of wudu, had wiped over khuffs, and now the period expires. Identify the act: removing the khuffs and what follows.
The same ruling applies if the khuff is removed before the period ends.
2
State the Hanafi ruling
The Hanafi school holds that upon expiry (or early removal), the person must remove the khuffs, wash both feet, and then pray. No other part of wudu needs to be repeated.
This is the core of the lesson.
3
Contrast with opposing views
Imam al-Shafi'i demands a complete renewal of wudu. Ibn Abi Layla says no washing at all. Al-Hasan and Tawus say pray without washing feet. Compare each to see the range of ijtihad.
Understanding the difference helps avoid confusion in practice.
4
Apply the underlying principle
The ruling rests on the principle that the prior hadath becomes active on the feet only upon removal. Therefore, only the feet are affected. This explains why the rest of wudu remains valid.
This principle is key to remembering the ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب المسح على الخفين فإذا تمَّتِ المدةُ : نَزَع خَفَّيْه، وغَسَلَ رِجليه، وصلَّى، وليس عليه إعادة بقية الوضوء . قوله : (فإذا تمت المدة : نَزَعَ خفيه، وغَسَلَ رجليه، وصلى). وكذا إذا نزع قبل مضي المدة ؛ لأنه عند النزع يسري الحدث السابق إلى القدمين، فكأنه لم يغسلهما. * قوله: (وليس عليه إعادة بقية الوضوء). هذا احتراز عن قول الشافعي) ، فإنه يقول: عليه إعادة الوضوء. وقال ابن أبي ليلى: لا يعيد شيئاً من الوضوء. * قوله : فإذا تمت المدة: نزع خفيه، وغسل رجليه. وقال الحسن، وطاوس يصلي، ولا يغسل قدميه.
Chapter on wiping over the khuffs. When the period ends, he removes his khuffs, washes his feet, and prays, and he does not have to repeat the rest of the wudu. He said: (And he does not have to repeat the rest of the wudu). This is a precaution against the statement of al-Shafi'i, for he says: he must repeat the wudu. Ibn Abi Layla said: he does not repeat anything of the wudu. He said: When the period ends, he removes his khuffs and washes his feet. Al-Hasan and Tawus said: he prays and does not wash his feet.

Commentary
This passage type is a ruling with inter-madhhab dispute. The matn states the basic ruling: when the wiping period (one day and night for a resident) ends, the person must remove the khuffs, wash the feet, and then pray. The commentary expands by noting that the same applies if he removes the khuffs before the period ends, because the hadath that occurred before removal immediately extends to the feet, making it necessary to wash them. The next phrase clarifies that only the feet need washing; the rest of the wudu remains valid. This counters Imam al-Shafi'i, who held that the entire wudu is invalidated. Ibn Abi Layla went to the opposite extreme, saying nothing needs to be repeated. The commentary also mentions the view of Hasan al-Basri and Tawus, who held that one prays without washing the feet at all, which is weaker.

Key Terms
مدة (mudda): The prescribed period for wiping over khuffs: one day and night for a resident, three days and nights for a traveler.

D) Keep khuffs on and pray.

Key Takeaway
When the wiping period ends, the Hanafi school requires only washing the feet, not repeating the entire wudu.

Starting Wiping as Resident Then Traveling
Ruling on completing the traveler's period when starting as resident

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that rulings attached to time consider its end to determine the wiping period and distinguish it from the Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
When travel status changes mid-wiping, the end of the period determines the full period.

Imagine you put on leather socks (khuff) and wipe over them while you are a resident. Then, before completing one full day and night, you set out on a journey. How long may you continue wiping? The Hanafi school answers: three days and nights, the traveler's period. The Shafi'i school disagrees, insisting your initial status binds you. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi reasoning, which relies on a broader principle: the decisive moment for time-based rulings is the end of the time, not the beginning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The situation: a person begins wiping (i.e., makes the first wipe) while a resident (muqim). Before completing one day and night (the resident's full period), he travels (becomes a musafir). The question: does he continue as a resident (1 day total) or as a traveler (3 days total)?
The key variable is the change of status mid-period.
2
State the Hanafi ruling
The Hanafi text says: 'He wipes for the full three days and their nights.' That is, he completes the traveler's period from the start of his wiping, not just one day.
The ruling is clear: the traveler's period applies.
3
Identify the opposing view
Imam al-Shafi'i held that he cannot avail himself of the traveler's concession. Apparently, the Shafi'i position considers the status at the start of wiping as determinative.
The Shafi'i position is mentioned only briefly; the text focuses on the Hanafi principle.
4
Understand the Hanafi principle
The commentary explains the underlying rule: 'The consideration in rulings attached to time is its end.' Analogies: prayer obligation changes if one travels at the end of the time (shortened or full); puberty or conversion at the end of the time triggers obligation.
This is a general usul principle applied to this case.
5
Apply the analogy to wiping
Since the person is a traveler when the wiping period ends (after traveling), the end-of-time principle dictates that the traveler's period applies. The initial resident status does not lock him into the shorter period.
The practical consequence: he may wipe for up to 3 days.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ومن ابتدأ المسح وهو مقيم فسافر قبل تمام يوم وليلة: مسح تمام ثلاثة أيام ولياليها). وقال الشافعي: لا يجوز أن يمسح مسح المسافر. والأصل في هذا: أن المعتبر عندنا في الأحكام المتعلقة بالوقت: آخره، كالصلاة إذا سافر في آخر الوقت يصير فرضه ركعتين، وإن أقام فيه: ينقلب فرضه أربعاً، وكذا الصبي إذا بلغ في آخر الوقت، أو أسلم الكافر: تجب عليهما الصلاة.
He said: (Whoever begins wiping as a resident and then travels before completing one day and night: he wipes for the full three days and their nights). Al-Shafi'i said: It is not permissible for him to wipe the wipe of a traveler. The principle in this is that the consideration according to us in rulings related to time is its end, like prayer: if he travels at the end of the time, his obligation becomes two rak'ahs, and if he resides in it, his obligation reverts to four, and likewise the boy if he reaches puberty at the end of the time, or the disbeliever if he embraces Islam: prayer becomes obligatory upon them.
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage addresses the scenario of a resident who begins wiping (with the intention of the resident's one-day period) but then travels before completing one day and night. The Hanafi ruling is that he now completes the traveler's period of three days and nights. Imam al-Shafi'i disagreed, saying he cannot avail himself of the traveler's concession because he began as a resident. The commentary explains the underlying Hanafi principle: in rulings that depend on time, the decisive moment is the end of the time. Analogies are given: if a person is still a resident at the end of a prayer time but was traveling during that time, he prays full (4 rak'ahs); if he travels right before the end, he prays shortened. Similarly, a boy who reaches puberty (or a disbeliever who converts) at the end of the time must pray that prayer. Here, because the person is a traveler when the wiping period ends (after traveling), the traveler's period applies.

Key Terms
مقيم (muqim): A person who is not traveling; a resident, for whom the wiping period is one day and night.
مسافر (musafir): A traveler, for whom the wiping period is three days and nights.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Only one day and night.
B) Three days and nights.
C) He must stop and wash his feet immediately.
D) He completes the resident's period only.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, time-bound rulings (like the wiping period) are determined by the status at the end of the time, not the beginning.

Starting Wiping as Traveler Then Becoming Resident
Ruling for traveler becoming resident during wiping period

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for a traveler who becomes resident during the wiping period and apply it to determine whether removal or completion is required.

Core Idea
If a traveler becomes resident after wiping a day+night or more, he must remove khuffs and wash feet; if less, he completes the resident's period.

This passage from Al-Jawhara al-Nayra addresses a common scenario: a traveler has been wiping his khuffs and then becomes a resident. The ruling depends on how much time has passed. Understanding this helps avoid invalidating salah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage distinguishes between two cases: the traveler became resident after wiping a day and night or more, or after wiping less than that.
2
First scenario: removal and washing required
If he has wiped a day and night or more, he must immediately remove his khuffs and wash his feet. If he delays and prays, the prayer becomes invalid.
This is because the condition for the extended wiping period (travel) has ended.
3
Second scenario: complete the resident period
If he has wiped less than a day and night, he continues wiping until he completes the resident's period of one day and night, just as if he had been resident from the start.
The text states there is no disagreement on this second case within the Hanafi school.
4
Understand the underlying principle
The resident wiping period is fixed at one day and night. The traveler's period is three days and nights. When travel ends, the residue of the resident period must be observed if the traveler hasn't yet completed it.
This ensures that the resident never wipes more than his prescribed period.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ومن ابتدأ المسح وهو مسافر، ثم أقام : فإن كان مَسَحَ يوماً وليلة، أو أكثر : لزمه نَزْعُ حَفَّيْه، وغَسْلُ رجليه . وإن كان مَسَحَ أقل من يوم وليلة : تمم مَسَحَ يوم وليلة . * قوله: (ومن ابتدأ المسح وهو مسافر، ثم أقام)، يعني دخل مصره، أو نوى الإقامة، فإن كان مَسَحَ يوماً وليلة، أو أكثر: لزمه نزع خفيه، وغَسْلُ رجليه)، حتى لو كان ذلك وهو في الصلاة: فسدت. * قوله : (وإن كان مَسَحَ أقل من يوم وليلة : أتمَّ مَسَحَ يوم وليلة)، كما لو كان مقيماً في الابتداء، وهذا لا خلاف فيه.
He said: (Whoever begins wiping as a traveler, then becomes resident: if he has wiped a day and night, or more, then removal of his khuffs and washing of his feet are required of him. And if he has wiped less than a day and night, he completes wiping for a day and night). He said: (Whoever begins wiping as a traveler, then becomes resident), meaning he enters his town or intends to reside, then if he has wiped a day and night or more, removal of his khuffs and washing of his feet are required of him), even if that happens while he is in prayer: it becomes invalid. He said: (And if he has wiped less than a day and night, he completes wiping for a day and night), as if he were resident from the beginning, and there is no disagreement regarding this.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage presents a clear ruling with sub-cases. The matn states that a traveler who becomes resident (by entering his home town or intending residence) has two scenarios: 1) If he has already wiped for one day and night or more (i.e., at least the full resident's period), then he must immediately remove his khuffs and wash his feet. The commentary emphasizes that if this happens during prayer, the prayer becomes invalid because the condition of wiping is no longer present. 2) If he has wiped for less than one day and night, he continues wiping until he completes the resident's period of one day and night, as if he had been resident from the beginning. The commentary notes that this second case is agreed upon without dispute.

Key Terms
إقامة (iqamah): The state of being a resident, either by entering one's hometown or intending to stay for at least 15 days (according to Hanafi).
نزع (naz'): Removal of the khuffs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Remove khuffs immediately and wash feet.
B) Continue wiping until completing one day and night.
C) Continue wiping for the full three days.
D) He must repeat the entire wudu.

Key Takeaway
When a traveler becomes resident, the requirement to remove or continue wiping depends on whether he has already wiped one day and night or more.

Wiping over the Jormuq: Definition and Conditions
Defines jormuq and states conditions for wiping over it

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define jormuq and state the Hanafi conditions for wiping over it.

Core Idea
Wiping over a jormuq is valid only if worn in the same state of purity as the khuff.

This lesson examines the ruling on wiping over the jormuq, a thin sock worn over the leather khuff. The Hanafi school permits wiping over it under specific conditions that ensure continuity of purification. Understanding these conditions is essential for correctly performing wudu and maintaining prayer validity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the object
Understand what a jormuq is: a thin sock worn over the khuff, shorter in leg length. The text defines it as 'a khuff over a khuff'.
2
State the ruling
Determine the legal ruling: wiping over the jormuq is permissible (ja'iz) when conditions are met. It is a valid substitute for washing the feet in wudu.
3
Extract the first condition
Identify the first condition: no hadath (ritual impurity) may intervene between wearing the khuff in a state of purity and wearing the jormuq. The jormuq must be put on while the wudu that allowed wearing the khuff is still intact.
The text explicitly states: 'that a hadath does not occur between it and the khuff'.
4
Analyze the example
The text provides an example: one puts on khuffs after wudu, then before any hadath, puts on jormuqs. In this case, wiping over the jormuqs is permissible. If a hadath occurs after wearing the khuffs and before wearing the jormuqs, the condition is broken.
The example illustrates the first condition clearly.
5
Note the missing condition
The passage is incomplete; it begins discussing a scenario after hadath and wiping over khuffs but cuts off. The second condition likely addresses the case where one wipes over the khuffs first before putting on jormuqs. Recognize that the full ruling requires completion from other sources.
The source chunk ends mid-sentence, indicating a page break.

Traveler Becomes Resident While Wiping Over Khuff
Ruling on traveler who becomes resident during wiping period

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a traveler becomes a resident during the wiping period and apply it to similar scenarios.

Core Idea
If a traveler has wiped a day or more, residence ends wiping immediately; otherwise, he completes the resident period.

In this passage, the author addresses a specific scenario: a person begins wiping over khuff while traveling, then becomes a resident before the wiping period expires. The ruling depends on how much time has passed since the first wipe. This illustrates how travel-based concessions adjust to changed circumstances.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The scenario is: a traveler begins wiping over khuff, then becomes a resident (by entering his city or intending to stay).
The change in status triggers a potential end to the wiping concession.
2
Distinguish based on duration already wiped
Check how long the person has been wiping: a day and night (24 hours) or more, versus less than a day and night.
A day and night is the travel wiping period; the threshold is 24 hours.
3
State the consequence for each case
If wiped a day/night or more: must remove khuff and wash feet immediately (even if in prayer). If less: complete wiping for a full day and night as if resident from start.
The ruling is unanimous within the Hanafi school.
4
Note the analogy to prayer
The commentary draws an analogy to prayer: if a traveler settles at the end of prayer time, his obligation reverts to four rak'ahs; similarly, wiping adjusts to residency status.
This shows that many 'ibadat rulings shift based on status at the end of the time.
5
Apply the principle
In any similar scenario, identify the time already wiped and compare to the traveler's period (24 hours) to determine the obligation.

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific scenario in the chapter on wiping over khuffs (leather socks). The ruling is stated: if a person begins wiping the khuff while traveling and then becomes a resident (by entering his hometown or intending to stay), the consequence depends on how long he has already wiped. The matn states that if he has wiped a full day and night or more, he must remove the khuff and wash his feet. The commentary explains that this obligation is immediate; even if he is in prayer when he becomes a resident, the prayer becomes invalid because wiping is no longer permitted. If he has wiped less than a day and night, he completes wiping for a full day and night, as if he had been a resident from the start. This ruling is agreed upon. The commentary then draws an analogy to prayer: just as the obligation of prayer changes from four rak'ahs to two when traveling at the end of the time, and back to four if he settles before completing the prayer, similarly the wiping period is adjusted based on residency status. The examples of a child reaching puberty or a disbeliever embracing Islam at the end of the prayer time show that obligations begin when the cause is complete.

Conditions for Wiping Over the Jurmuk
States two necessary conditions for wiping over a jurmuk worn over a khuff

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two conditions required for wiping over a jurmuk and apply them to determine whether a given scenario permits wiping over the jurmuk.

Core Idea
Wiping over a jurmuk is only valid if the underlying khuff was never wiped after a hadath.

This lesson unpacks the ruling on wiping over a jurmuk, a short outer boot worn over the khuff. The Hanafi school permits this practice, but only under two strict conditions that preserve the integrity of the original khuff's wiping. Understanding these conditions will prevent common errors when multiple layers are worn.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act under discussion is wiping over a jurmuk (outer boot) during wudu. The ruling is that it is permissible, but only if two conditions are met.
The jurmuk is defined as a boot shorter than the khuff, worn over it.
2
List the two conditions
Condition 1: No hadath (ritual impurity) occurs between wearing the khuff and putting on the jurmuk. Condition 2: The jurmuk itself must be suitable for wiping if worn alone (i.e., no large holes).
3
Analyze Condition 1 in detail
The key is that the khuff must be worn in a state of purity, and the jurmuk must be put on before that purity is broken. If the khuff is wiped first, the ruling of wiping becomes fixed on the khuff, and wiping over the jurmuk is no longer valid. Similarly, if hadath occurs after wearing the khuff but before wiping, then the jurmuk is worn in a state of impurity, invalidating the wipe.
The text gives three scenarios: proper sequence, wiping khuff first, and hadath before wiping.
4
Analyze Condition 2 in detail
The jurmuk itself must meet the same conditions as a regular khuff for wiping: it must cover the foot up to the ankle and be free of large holes. If the jurmuk has a large tear, wiping over it is not permitted, even if worn over a sound khuff.
5
Apply to scenarios
Practice distinguishing valid and invalid sequences. For example: wearing khuff in wudu, then jurmuk, then hadath → valid to wipe jurmuk. Wearing khuff, then hadath, then jurmuk → invalid because purity was broken before wearing jurmuk.
The practical consequence is that one must plan the order of wearing when using multiple layers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
* قوله : (وَمَن لَبِسَ الجُرموق فوق الخف: مَسَحَ عليه). الجُرموق : خُفٌ فوق حُفٌ، إلا أن ساقه أقصر منه، وإنما يجوز المسح عليه بشرطين :
١- أحدهما: أن يتخلل بينه وبين الخف حدث، كما إذا لبس الخفين على طهارة ولم يمسح عليهما حتى لبس الجرموقين قبل أن تنتقض الطهارة التي لبس عليها الخف : فحينئذ يجوز المسح على الجرموقين. وأما إذا أحدث بعد لبس الخفين، ومَسَحَ عليهما، ثم لبس الجرموقين بعد ذلك: لا يجوز له المسح على الجرموقين؛ لأن حكم المسح قد استقر على الخف. وكذا لو أحدث بعد لبس الخف، ثم لبس الجرموق قبل أن يمسح على الخف: لا يمسح عليه أيضاً.
٢- والشرط الثاني: أن يكون الجرموق لو انفرد جاز المسح عليه، حتى لو كان به خُرق كبير: لا يجوز المسح عليه.
His saying: (Whoever wears the jurmuk over the khuff: he wipes over it). The jurmuk is a khuff over a khuff, except that its leg is shorter than it. It is only permissible to wipe over it with two conditions: 1- One of them: that a hadath (impurity) occurs between it and the khuff, such as if he wears the two khuffs in a state of purity and does not wipe over them until he wears the two jarmuqin before the purity on which he wore the khuff is nullified: then it is permissible to wipe over the two jarmuqin. But if he becomes impure after wearing the two khuffs, and wipes over them, then wears the two jarmuqin after that: it is not permissible for him to wipe over the two jarmuqin, because the ruling of wiping has been established on the khuff. Similarly, if he becomes impure after wearing the khuff, then wears the jurmuk before wiping over the khuff: he does not wipe over it either. 2- The second condition: that the jurmuk, if alone, would be permissible to wipe over it; so if it has a large hole, it is not permissible to wipe over it.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling on wiping over a jurmuk, an outer boot worn over the khuff. The commentator defines the jurmuk as a boot that is shorter in the leg than the khuff. Two conditions are necessary for wiping over the jurmuk. First, there must be no hadath (ritual impurity) between wearing the khuff and the jurmuk; that is, one must wear the khuff in a state of purity, and then wear the jurmuk before the purity is broken. If one wipes the khuff first, the obligation of wiping is fixed on the khuff, making wiping over the jurmuk invalid. Similarly, if one breaks purity before wiping the khuff, then wears the jurmuk, wiping over the jurmuk is not allowed. Second, the jurmuk itself must be a valid covering for wiping: if it were worn alone, it must meet the conditions for wiping (e.g., no large holes). If the jurmuk has a large tear, wiping over it is not permitted even when worn over a khuff.

Key Terms
جُرموق (jurmuk): A boot worn over the khuff, shorter in the leg than the khuff.
حدث (hadath): Ritual impurity that nullifies wudu, such as passing wind, urine, etc.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Becoming impure after wearing the khuff, then wearing the jurmuk before wiping the khuff.
D) Wearing the jurmuk with a large hole over a sound khuff.

Key Takeaway
Wiping over a jurmuk is valid only if the khuff was never wiped after a hadath and the jurmuk itself has no large hole.

Wiping Over Socks: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
States the ikhtilaf on wiping over ordinary socks with conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on wiping over ordinary socks (jurab) and explain the conditions that make wiping permissible in each view.

Core Idea
Wiping over socks requires leather or thickness: Abu Hanifa requires leather reinforcement; Sahibayn accept thick socks.

In this lesson, we examine an intra-Hanafi dispute about wiping over ordinary socks during wudu. Imam Abu Hanifa restricts wiping to socks that are either lined with leather (mujallad) or have a leather sole (mun'all), arguing that ordinary socks cannot be walked on and thus resemble a bandage. The two Sahibayn, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, hold a broader view, allowing wiping over socks that are thick enough to be walked in. Understanding this difference is essential for applying the correct ruling in daily life and appreciating the role of condition-setting in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act in dispute
The act is wiping over jurab (socks) during wudu instead of washing the feet. This is a sub-issue of the rules for khuff (leather socks).
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and its condition
Abu Hanifa says wiping is not permissible unless the socks are mujallad (leather-lined on top and bottom) or mun'all (having a leather sole). His reasoning: ordinary socks cannot be walked on normally, so they resemble a bandage (lifafa), not a proper khuff.
The Arabic terms mujallad and mun'all are technical conditions.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and implied condition
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say wiping is permissible over ordinary socks if they are thick enough to be walked in. Their condition is based on functionality rather than material.
The source text cuts off before stating their full condition, but classical Hanafi works fill in that they require thickness.
4
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
A person wearing ordinary thick cotton socks can wipe over them according to the Sahibayn but not according to Abu Hanifa, because the socks lack leather reinforcement.
This difference directly affects the validity of wudu in daily practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يجوز المسح على الجَوْربين عند أبي حنيفة إلا أن يكونا مجلدين أو منعلين .
وقال أبو يوسف ومحمد : يجوز المسح على الجوربين إذا
And wiping over the two jurabs is not permissible according to Abu Hanifa unless they are lined with leather or soled with leather. And Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: Wiping over the two jurabs is permissible if...
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding wiping over jurabs (socks). According to Imam Abu Hanifa, wiping over ordinary socks is not allowed unless the socks are either 'mujallad' (lined with leather on the top and bottom) or 'mun'all' (having a leather sole attached, like a sandal). The reasoning given in the fuller text is that ordinary socks cannot be walked upon in the normal way, so they resemble a bandage, not a proper khuff. However, if they have leather reinforcement, they become similar to khuffs. The two Saahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold that wiping over socks is permissible with some condition, though the text cuts off before stating their condition. Typically, the Sahibayn's position is that socks are permissible for wiping if they are thick and can be walked in, even without leather. This ikhtilaf has practical implications: in the Hanafi school, the mufta bihi (relied upon) position in later centuries is often the one allowing wiping on thick socks, but the text here only reports the disagreement.

Key Terms
جَوْرَب (jurab): A sock or stocking worn on the foot, typically made of cloth or yarn.
مجلد (mujallad): A sock that has leather attached to both its top and bottom.
منعل (mun'all): A sock that has a leather sole attached to its bottom, like a sandal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The permissibility of wiping over socks in the Hanafi school depends on their condition: Abu Hanifa requires leather reinforcement, while the Sahibayn accept sufficient thickness; the later fatwa often adopts the Sahibayn's view for practical ease.

Condition of Thickness for Wiping over Khuffs
Definition of thickness for khuffs with intra-madhhab note

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi condition for a khuff being thick (thakhin) and explain its definition as self-standing and impermeable.

Core Idea
A khuff must be thick enough to stand on its own and not reveal the skin for wiping to be valid.

Wiping over leather socks (khuffs) is a concession in wudu, but it has conditions. The most basic is that the khuff must be 'thick' – a term with a precise legal definition. This lesson unpacks that definition and the intra-madhhab history that settled the fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the required attribute
Find the explicit condition in the text: 'they must be thick (thakhinayn)'. Note that this is a condition for the permissibility of wiping.
The Arabic verb 'yushiffan' (transmit water) indicates impermeability.
2
Analyze the definition of thickness
The text defines two criteria: (1) the khuff stands upright on its own without ties, and (2) it does not reveal the skin underneath. These define 'thakhin' in legal terms.
This is a functional definition, not based on material.
3
Recognize the variant reading and correction
The commentary corrects a common textual error: 'yanishafan' (absorb) is wrong; the correct is 'yushiffan' (transmit). The condition is that water does not go through.
This affects the meaning: impermeability vs. absorption.
4
Trace the intra-madhhab resolution
The passage from Al-Dhakhirah reports that Abu Hanifah retracted to the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad before his death. The fatwa is now upon the condition of thickness.
This shows the evolution of the school's position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب المسح على الخفين
كانا ثخينين لا يشفان الماء.
(۱) وفي نسخ من القدوري: لا يشفان الماء.
حد الثخانة : أن يقوم على الساق من غير أن يُربط بشيء. وقوله : لا يشفان : أي لا يُرى ما تحتهما من بشرة الرجل من خلاله. و: «ينشفان»: خطأ.
قال في «الذخيرة»: رجع أبو حنيفة إلى قولهما في آخر عمره قبل موته بسبعة أيام، وقيل: بثلاثة أيام، وعليه الفتوى.
Chapter on wiping over the khuffs. They must be thick, not transmitting water. (Footnote: And in some copies of Al-Quduri: 'they do not transmit water.') The definition of thickness: that they stay upright on the shin without being tied with anything. And his statement: 'they do not transmit' means that the skin of the foot underneath them is not seen through them. And (the reading) 'yanishafan' (they absorb) is incorrect. It is stated in Al-Dhakhirah: Abu Hanifah retracted to their (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) view in the last part of his life, seven days before his death, and it is said three days, and upon it is the fatwa.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage defines the condition for the validity of wiping over leather socks (khuffs): they must be thick (thakhin) and impermeable. The thickness is defined as the khuff standing on its own without ties, and impermeability means the skin is not visible through the leather. The variant reading 'yanishafan' (they absorb) is a common error. The commentary from Al-Dhakhirah records that Imam Abu Hanifah retracted his earlier view and adopted the position of his two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) shortly before his death, and this retraction is the basis for the fatwa. Thus, the relied-upon ruling in the Hanafi school requires thickness for valid wiping.

Key Terms
خفين (khuffain): Leather socks or boots that cover the feet up to the ankles, over which wiping is allowed in wudu.
ثخين (thakhin): Thick, specifically meaning the khuff can stand upright on its own without being tied and does not reveal the skin through the leather.
الذخيرة (Al-Dhakhirah): A Hanafi fiqh work by Imam al-Marghinani, used as a source for the retraction narration.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the definition of thickness (thakhin) for khuffs according to this passage?
A) That they are made of leather.
B) That they stay upright on the shin without being tied and do not reveal the skin.
C) That they are waterproof and can absorb water.
D) That they cover the entire leg.

Key Takeaway
Wiping over khuffs requires thickness – the khuff must stand on its own and be impermeable – and this is the relied-upon Hanafi position after Abu Hanifah's retraction.

Items Over Which Wiping is Not Permitted: Prohibition and Rationale
States prohibition of wiping over turban, cap, face veil, and gloves

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the prohibition of wiping over specific head and arm coverings and explain the underlying rationale of hardship (haraj).

Core Idea
Wiping over a turban or gloves is prohibited because removing them causes no hardship.

In this lesson, we examine a clear ruling from the Hanafi school: wiping over items like the turban, cap, face veil, and gloves is not permissible during wudu. The author provides the rationale that the concession of wiping over coverings is only granted when removal involves hardship. Since these items are easily taken off, the ruling returns to the default obligation of washing the limb directly. Understanding this principle helps distinguish between permitted and prohibited forms of wiping.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is wiping over certain garments during wudu. The ruling is that it is not permissible (haram).
2
List the specific items
These include the turban (imamah), cap (qalansuwah), face veil (burqu'), and gloves (quffazayn).
3
Understand the rationale (illa)
The text states that there is no hardship (haraj) in removing these items, and the concession (rukhsah) is only to remove hardship.
The principle 'الرخصة إنما هي لرفع الحرج' is a key legal maxim.
4
Distinguish from cases where wiping is permitted
For example, wiping over khuffs is permitted because removing them repeatedly causes hardship. The difference lies in the presence or absence of hardship.
5
Apply the principle to new cases
If an item is difficult to remove due to extreme cold or injury, wiping may be permissible; otherwise, the prohibition stands.
The ruling is conditional on the absence of hardship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ولا يجوز المسح على العمامة، والقلنسوة، والبرقع، والقفازين)؛ لأنه لا حرج في نزع هذه الأشياء، والرخصة إنما هي لرفع الحرج. القلنسوة: شيء تجعله الأعاجم على رؤوسهم، أكبر من الكوفية. والبرقع : شيء تجعله المرأة على وجهها، تبدو منه العينان. والقفاز: شيء يُجعل على الذراعين، يُحشى قطناً، له أزرار، يُلبسان من شدة البرد.
His statement: (And it is not permissible to wipe over the turban, the cap, the face veil, and the gloves) because there is no hardship in removing these things, and the concession is only to remove hardship. The qalansuwah: something that the non-Arabs put on their heads, larger than the kufiyyah. The burqu': something a woman puts on her face, from which the eyes appear. And the quffaz: something placed on the forearms, stuffed with cotton, with buttons, worn due to extreme cold.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage states a clear prohibition: wiping over a turban, cap, face veil, or gloves is not allowed. The underlying reason (illa) is that there is no hardship (haraj) in removing these items before washing the head or arms during wudu. The concession (rukhsah) of wiping over headgear was granted for cases of hardship, such as with khuffs, but not for these easily removable items. The commentator then defines each term to avoid confusion: the qalansuwah is a large cap worn by non-Arabs, the burqu' is a face veil showing the eyes, and the quffaz are cotton-stuffed gloves with buttons worn in extreme cold. This helps apply the ruling correctly to various forms of head and arm coverings.

Key Terms
عمامة (imamah): A turban worn on the head, often wrapped around a cap.
قلنسوة (qalansuwah): A type of cap larger than a kufiyyah, worn by non-Arabs.
برقع (burqu'): A face veil worn by women that covers the lower part of the face, exposing the eyes.
قفاز (quffaz): Gloves, often stuffed with cotton and buttoned, worn for protection from cold.
حرج (haraj): Hardship or difficulty, used here as the basis for granting the concession of wiping over headgear.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Wiping over a turban, cap, face veil, or gloves is prohibited in the Hanafi school because there is no hardship in removing them before washing.

Ruling on Wiping over Splints (Jabirah)
Details permissibility and conditions of wiping over splints

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the permissibility of wiping over splints, identify the conditions and four differences from wiping over khuffs, and apply the principle of tabi' (followership) to medical coverings.

Core Idea
Wiping over splints is permissible even without prior wudu, differing from khuffs in four key ways.

This passage from al-Jawharah al-Nayirah details the Hanafi ruling on wiping over splints (jabirah) used for fractures or wounds. It establishes the basic permissibility and then contrasts it with the well-known rules for wiping over leather socks (khuffs). Understanding these differences is essential for correctly applying taharah rulings in medical contexts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what act is being ruled upon (wiping over splints) and its hukm level (permissible). Note that the text begins with 'wa-yajuz al-mash 'ala al-jaba'ir'.
The ruling is established in the zahir al-riwayah.
2
Determine the conditions for permissibility
Identify the conditions: the splint is applied to a fracture or wound; washing would cause harm (according to Abu Ali al-Nasafi); and it is valid whether tightened with or without wudu.
The condition of harm is an additional requirement from later Hanafi scholarship.
3
List the four differences from khuffs
Work through each of the four points: (1) falling after healing – wash only the spot; (2) falling before healing – retighten without repeating wipe; (3) no time limit for wiping; (4) tightening without wudu is valid.
These distinctions highlight the flexibility of the ruling for medical necessities.
4
Apply the principle of tabi' (followership)
If part of the splint covers healthy skin, it is considered a follower (tabi') of the wound, so wiping over the entire splint is permissible.
This principle extends to any inseparable medical covering.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ويجوز المسح على الجبائر).
الجبائر : عيدان يُجبَر بها الكسر.
وإن شَدَّها على غير وضوء .
وأجري الحكم فيما إذا شدَّها بخرقة، أو انكسر ظفره، فجعل عليه العلك أو الدواء: مجرى ذلك.
والمحدث والجنب في مسح الجبيرة: سواء.
قوله : (وإن شدها على غير وضوء).
اعلم أنها تخالف المسح على الخفين بأربعة أشياء:
١- أحدها: أنها إذا سقطت عن بُرء : يُكتفى بغسل ذلك الموضع، بخلاف الخفين، فإن أحدهما إذا سقط : يجب غسل الرجلين.
والثاني: إذا سقطت على غير بُرء : شدَّها مرةً أخرى، ولا يجب عليه إعادة المسح.
والثالث: أن مسحها لا يتوقت.
والرابع : إذا شدَّها على طهارة، أو على غير طهارة: يجوز المسح عليها، بخلاف الخفين.
قال أبو علي النسفي : إنما يجوز المسح على الجبيرة إذا كان المسح على الجراحة يضره، وإلا: فلا يجوز.
ويجوز المسح على الجبيرة وإن كان بعضها على الصحيح، ويكون تبعاً للمجروح ؛ لأنه لا يمكن شد الجبيرة على الجرح خاصة.
His statement: (And it is permissible to wipe over splints). The splints: sticks by which a fracture is set. And if he tightened them without wudu. And the ruling is extended to when he tightens them with a cloth, or his nail breaks and he puts gum or medicine on it: it is treated in the same way. And the one with minor ritual impurity and the one in major impurity are equal regarding wiping over the splint. His statement: (And if he tightened them without wudu). Know that it differs from wiping over the khuffs in four matters: 1- One: if they fall off after healing, it suffices to wash that spot, unlike the khuffs, because if one of them falls off, it becomes obligatory to wash the feet. 2- Second: if they fall off before healing, he retightens them again, and it is not obligatory for him to repeat the wiping. 3- Third: its wiping is not time-limited. 4- Fourth: if he tightened them with taharah or without taharah, it is permissible to wipe over them, unlike the khuffs. Abu Ali al-Nasafi said: It is only permissible to wipe over the splint if wiping over the wound would harm him; otherwise, it is not permissible. And it is permissible to wipe over the splint even if part of it is over healthy skin, and it is considered a follower of the wounded area, because it is not possible to tighten the splint on the wound alone.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage covers the rules for wiping over splints (jabirah) used for fractures or wounds. The key points: wiping is permissible, and the splint is defined as sticks used to set bones. The ruling applies even if tightened without wudu, and it extends to similar situations like wrapping with a cloth or applying medicine to broken nails. Both a person in minor impurity (muhdith) and major impurity (junub) are treated equally. The passage then lists four differences between splints and khuffs: (1) if the splint falls off after healing, only washing the healed spot is needed, whereas if a khuff falls, the entire foot must be washed; (2) if the splint falls before healing, retightening suffices without repeating the wipe; (3) splint wiping has no time limit; (4) tightening with or without wudu is valid for splints, unlike khuffs. A condition from Abu Ali al-Nasafi is added: wiping is only allowed if washing the wound would cause harm; otherwise, it is not permitted. Finally, if part of the splint covers healthy skin, it is considered secondary (tabi') to the wound, as it cannot be separated. This shows the fiqh principle of treating inseparable items as followers.

Key Terms
جبائر (jaba'ir): Splints used to set fractures, typically sticks or rigid materials applied to the broken bone area.
بُرء (bur'): Healing or recovery from injury, after which the splint may fall off.
محدث (muhdith): A person in a state of minor ritual impurity (hadath asghar), requiring wudu.
جنب (junub): A person in a state of major ritual impurity (hadath akbar), requiring ghusl.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The entire limb must be washed.
B) It suffices to wash only the spot that was under the splint.
C) The person must retighten the splint and repeat wiping.
D) The person is excused from washing altogether.

Key Takeaway
Wiping over splints is permissible, differs from khuffs in four ways, and part over healthy skin follows the wound.

Rules of Wiping over Bandages (Jabirah)
Stating the rulings of wiping over bandages and its differences from khuffs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four differences between wiping over bandages (jabirah) and wiping over khuffs, and apply the rulings when the bandage falls off before or after healing.

Core Idea
Wiping over bandages is more lenient than wiping over khuffs in four ways: no prior wudu needed, no time limit, and different consequences when it falls off.

This lesson examines the Hanafi rulings for wiping over bandages (jabirah) during purification. The text compares bandage wiping to khuff wiping, highlighting four key differences that make bandage wiping more flexible. Understanding these differences ensures correct practice when dealing with wounds and medical dressings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what is being ruled upon: wiping over a bandage (jabirah) during wudu or ghusl. The hukm is permissible (mubah) under the condition that washing the wound would cause harm.
The ruling applies to both minor (hadath) and major (janabah) impurity.
2
List the four differences from khuffs
The text lists four points: (1) If bandage falls after healing, only wash the spot; if khuff falls, wash both feet. (2) If bandage falls before healing, retie without repeating wipe; if khuff falls, wash feet. (3) Bandage wiping has no time limit; khuff wiping is limited to one day/night for resident, three for traveler. (4) Bandage wiping is allowed even if tied without wudu; khuff wiping requires wudu at the time of putting them on.
These differences show bandage rulings are more lenient.
3
Understand the condition for permissibility
Wiping over the bandage is only allowed if washing the wound would cause harm. If no harm, one must wash the wound. This is a crucial condition.
Also, if the bandage partially covers healthy skin, wiping over it is allowed because the bandage necessarily covers some healthy area.
4
Apply rulings when the bandage falls off
If it falls before healing (excuse remains): the wipe remains valid. If during prayer, continue. If outside prayer, retie and pray without repeating wipe. If it falls after healing (excuse ends): the wipe is invalidated. Wash the spot before prayer. If during prayer, wash and restart prayer.
Analogous to tayammum: when able to use water (original), the substitute (tayammum/wipe) ends.
5
Note the analogy to tayammum
The text explicitly compares to one performing tayammum who finds water during prayer: he must wash and restart. Similarly, if the bandage falls after healing during prayer, wash and restart.
This highlights the principle that the substitute is valid only as long as the excuse persists.

والمحدث والجنب في مسح الجبيرة: سواء.

Chapter of Wiping over Khuffs. And if he tied them without wudū'. Al-Jabā'ir: sticks by which a fracture is set. And the ruling is applied in the case where he tied it with a cloth, or his nail broke and he applied gum or medicine on it: it takes the same ruling. And the one in minor impurity (muḥdith) and the one in major impurity (junub) are the same regarding wiping over the bandage. * His statement: (And if he tied it without wudū'). Know that it differs from wiping over khuffs in four matters: 1. One: that if it falls off after healing, washing that spot suffices, unlike khuffs, for if one of them falls, washing both feet is obligatory. 2. The second: if it falls off before healing, he ties it again, and he does not have to repeat the wipe. 3. The third: its wipe is not time-limited. 4. The fourth: whether he tied it while in a state of purity or without purity, wiping over it is permissible, unlike khuffs. Abu Ali al-Nasafi said: Wiping over the bandage is only permissible if wiping over the wound would harm it; otherwise, it is not permissible. And it is permissible to wipe over the bandage even if some of it is on healthy skin, as it follows the wounded part because it is not possible to tie the bandage on the wound only. If it falls off before healing, the wipe is not invalidated. If it falls off after healing, the wipe is invalidated. And accordingly, for the bandage of the one who has undergone phlebotomy, he should wipe over the entire bandage as long as the mouth of the vein is not closed. His statement: (And if it falls off before healing, the wipe is not invalidated) because the excuse remains. His statement: (And if it falls off after healing, it is invalidated) because the excuse ceases. If it falls off after healing while he is in prayer, he washes that spot and restarts the prayer, because he became able to use the original before achieving the purpose through the substitute, like the one performing tayammum when he finds water during his prayer. And if it falls off before healing while he is in prayer, he continues his prayer, because the ruling of wiping remains due to the persistence of the cause. And if it falls off before healing while he is not in prayer, he ties it again and prays, and he does not have to repeat the wipe, whether he ties it with those same bandages or with others. And if it falls off after healing, he washes that spot and prays, and he is not allowed to pray until he washes it.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage discusses the rulings of wiping over bandages (جبائر) placed on fractures or wounds. It begins by stating that if one ties the bandage without being in wudu, wiping over it is still permissible. The text defines الجبائر as splints used to set broken bones. The same ruling applies if the bandage is a cloth or if something like gum or medicine is applied to a broken nail. Both the one with minor impurity (حدث) and major impurity (جنب) are equal in this ruling, meaning both can wipe over the bandage when performing wudu or ghusl. The author then lists four differences between bandage wiping and wiping over khuffs: (1) if the bandage falls off after healing, only washing that spot suffices, whereas if a khuff falls, washing both feet is required; (2) if the bandage falls off before healing, one reties it without repeating the wipe, while for a khuff that falls, one must wash the feet; (3) bandage wiping has no time limit, whereas khuff wiping is limited to one day for a resident and three for a traveler; (4) bandage wiping is allowed even if tied without wudu, while khuff wiping requires being in wudu when putting them on. Abu Ali al-Nasafi adds a condition: wiping is only permitted if wiping the wound itself would be harmful; otherwise, one must wash it. Also, if the bandage partially covers healthy skin, wiping over it is allowed because the bandage necessarily covers some healthy area. The text then discusses cases where the bandage falls off: if it falls before healing, the wipe remains valid; if after healing, the wipe is invalidated. If it falls after healing during prayer, one must wash the spot and restart the prayer, analogous to a mutayammim finding water. If it falls before healing during prayer, one continues. If it falls before healing outside prayer, one reties it and prays without repeating the wipe. If it falls after healing, one must wash the spot before praying. The text also mentions the bandage for a cut vein (عصابة المقتصد): one wipes the entire bandage as long as the wound is open.

Key Terms
الجبائر (al-jabā'ir): Splints or bandages used to set broken bones, here extended to any covering for a wound or injury that requires protection from water during purification.
العصابة (al-iṣābah): A bandage, specifically one used to cover a cut vein or artery (as in phlebotomy), where the entire bandage is wiped until the wound closes.
البرء (al-bur'): Healing or recovery, the point at which the wound is no longer an excuse for wiping over the bandage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Wash the spot and restart the prayer from the beginning.
C) Tie the bandage again and continue praying.
D) Continue the prayer without any action because the wipe is still valid.

Key Takeaway
Wiping over bandages is more lenient than wiping over khuffs in four ways, and the key condition is the presence of an excuse (harm from washing).

6 min
Rulings on the Bandage Falling Off a Wound
Expands conditions for invalidation of bandage wiping with analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between bandage falling with and without healing, and apply the Hanafi ruling on invalidation of wiping using qiyas to tayammum.

Core Idea
A bandage's wiping is only invalidated if healing has occurred; otherwise, the excuse remains.

The Hanafi school addresses the scenario where a bandage or splint falls off during wudu or prayer. The key factor is whether the wound has healed. This lesson unpacks the conditions that trigger invalidation, using the analogy of tayammum to strengthen the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is wiping over a bandage (jabirah). The ruling varies based on whether the bandage falls off due to healing or not.
Falling off without healing does not invalidate; falling off with healing invalidates.
2
Understand the rationale: cause (illah) of the ruling
The ruling hinges on the presence of an excuse (ʿudhr). If the excuse remains (wound unhealed), wiping stays valid. If healing occurs, the excuse ceases.
The illah is the existence of the wound preventing direct washing.
3
Apply the analogy to tayammum
The author cites the case of tayammum: finding water during prayer invalidates tayammum and necessitates restart. Similarly, ability to wash the wound during prayer due to healing invalidates the bandage wiping.
Qiyas: both are substitutes that lose validity when the original becomes accessible.
4
Note the practical scenarios
The passage distinguishes four scenarios: in prayer or not, and with or without healing. Each has a different consequence.
Key: in prayer with healing -> wash spot and restart; without healing -> continue.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi proof
The proof is qiyas jaliy on tayammum, which is a strong analogy. No other textual evidence is cited; the school relies on this rational derivation.
Other schools may differ; but within Hanafi, this is the relied-upon view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب المسح على الخفين
فإن سقطت عن غير بُرء : لم يبطل المسح.
وإن سقطت عن بُرء : بطل المسح.
وعلى هذا عصابة المقتصد له أن يمسح على جميع العصابة ما لم ينفسد فم العرق.
قوله: (وإن سقطت عن غير بُرء : لم يبطل المسح)؛ لأن العذر قائم.
قوله : (وإن سقطت عن بُرء: بطل)؛ لزوال العذر.
فلو سقطت عن برء وهو في الصلاة: غسل ذلك الموضع، واستقبل الصلاة؛ لأنه قدر على الأصل قبل حصول المقصود بالبدل، كالمتيمم إذا وجد الماء في خلال صلاته.
وإن كان سقوطها عن غير بُرء وهو في الصلاة: مضى على صلاته؛ لأن حكم المسح باق؛ لبقاء العلة.
وإن سقطت عن غير برء وهو في غير الصلاة: شدَّها مرةً أخرى، ويصلي، ولا تجب عليه إعادة المسح، سواء شدَّها بتلك الجبائر، أو بغيرها.
وإن سقطت عن برء: فإنه يغسل ذلك الموضع، ويصلي، ولا يجوز له أن يصلي ما لم يغسله
Chapter of wiping over the two leather socks (khuffs). If it (the bandage) falls off without healing: wiping is not invalidated. If it falls off due to healing: wiping is invalidated. And upon this is the bandage of the frugal person: he may wipe over the entire bandage as long as the opening of the wound does not become blocked. His statement: (And if it falls off without healing: wiping is not invalidated) because the excuse remains. His statement: (And if it falls off due to healing: it is invalidated) because the excuse ceases. So if it falls off due to healing while he is in prayer: he washes that spot and restarts the prayer; because he was able to use the original (water) before the goal was achieved by the substitute, like the one who performs tayammum when he finds water during his prayer. If it falls off without healing while he is in prayer: he continues his prayer; because the ruling of wiping remains due to the persistence of the cause. If it falls off without healing while he is not in prayer: he ties it again and prays, and he is not required to repeat the wiping, whether he ties it with those splints or others. If it falls off due to healing: then he washes that spot and prays, and it is not permissible for him to pray unless he washes it.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Hanafi school regarding the invalidation of wiping on a bandage (jabirah) when it falls off. The author first states the general rule: if the bandage falls off without the wound healing, the wiping remains valid because the excuse (the wound) still exists. If it falls off due to healing, wiping is invalidated because the excuse has ceased. He then applies this to different scenarios. If healing occurs during prayer, the person must wash the affected area and restart the prayer, analogous to a person performing tayammum who finds water during prayer—both were using a substitute and became able to use the original. If it falls off without healing during prayer, the prayer continues because the ruling of wiping persists. Outside of prayer, if it falls without healing, one simply reties the bandage and prays without needing to re-wipe; this applies whether the same or different splints are used. If it falls due to healing outside of prayer, one must wash the area before praying. The passage is from a sharh (commentary) expanding on the matn, using qiyas (analogy) to tayammum to strengthen the ruling.

Key Terms
عصابة (ʿiṣābah): A bandage or wrapping applied to a wound.
جبائر (jabāʾir): Splints used to set bones; also used generically for bandages.
برء (burʾ): Healing or recovery from a wound.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A person has a bandage on a wound and performs wudu by wiping over it. During prayer, the bandage falls off due to the wound healing. What must he do?
A) Continue the prayer, as the bandage was valid at the start.
B) Wash the wound and restart the prayer.
C) Simply retie the bandage and continue.
D) Make up the prayer later but continue now.

Key Takeaway
Wiping on a bandage is invalidated only when the wound heals and the bandage falls off; otherwise, the wiping remains valid.

Definition of Menstruation (Hayd) in Hanafi Fiqh
Defines hayd linguistically and technically with conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the linguistic and technical definition of menstruation (hayd) and identify its five conditions according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Hanafi fiqh defines hayd as human vaginal blood lasting 3-10 days, excluding illness and extremes of age.

This passage from the chapter on menstruation (Bāb al-Ḥayḍ) clarifies why the author placed it after the discussion of major and minor impurities. It then provides both a linguistic and a technical (sharʿī) definition of hayd, emphasizing conditions that distinguish it from istihāḍah (chronic bleeding). Understanding these conditions is essential for correctly applying rulings on purification, prayer, and fasting.

Guided Path
1
Placement in the text
Note why hayd is discussed before nifas: because hayd occurs more frequently (حيث أن الحيض أكثر وقوعاً).
This structure helps prioritize common issues in fiqh.
2
Linguistic definition
Understand the broad literal meaning: any blood from the vulva, even animals (e.g., hare). This includes non-menstrual bleeding.
Linguistic scope is wider; technical scope is narrower.
3
Technical definition - five restrictions
Break down the five restrictions: 1) specific blood (human), 2) specific exit (vagina), 3) specific person (not pre-pubescent or menopausal), 4) specific time (menstrual age), 5) specific duration (3-10 days).
Each restriction excludes a type of bleeding that would otherwise be linguistically called hayd.
4
Alternative technical phrasing
The additional phrasing 'blood from a woman free from disease and young age' reinforces the condition of health and maturity.
Disease-related bleeding is istihāḍah, not hayd.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الحيض
لما قدَّم ذِكْرَ الأحداث التي يكثُر وقوعها، من الأصغر والأكبر، والأحكام المتعلقة بها أصلاً وخَلَفاً : ذَكَرَ عَقيبه حكم الأحداث التي يقل وجودها، وهو الحيض والنفاس. ولهذا المعنى قَدَّمَ ذِكْرَ الحيض على النفاس؛ لأن الحيض أكثر وقوعاً منه. والحيض في اللغة: اسمٌ لخروج الدم من الفرج على أي صفة كان، من آدمية أو غيرها، حتى قالوا: حاضت الأرنبة: إذا خرج من فرجها الدم. وفي الشرع: عبارة عن دم مخصوص، أي دم بنات آدم. من مخرج مخصوص، وهو موضع الولادة. من شخص مخصوص؛ احترازاً عن الصغيرة، والآيسة. في وقت مخصوص، وهو أن يكون في أوانه - يمتد مدة مخصوصة، أي لا يزيد على العشر، ولا ينقص عن الثلاث. ويقال في تفسيره شرعاً أيضاً: هو الدم الخارج من رحم امرأة سليمة من الداء، والصغر.
Chapter of menstruation. When he (the author) presented the mention of the events that frequently occur, from the minor and major, and the rulings related to them originally and as substitutes, he mentioned after it the rulings of events that rarely occur, which are menstruation and postpartum bleeding. And for this reason he presented the mention of menstruation before postpartum bleeding because menstruation occurs more frequently than it. And menstruation in the language: a name for the exit of blood from the vulva in whatever description it may be, from a human female or other, such that they said: 'the hare menstruates' when blood exits from its vulva. And in the Sharee'ah: an expression for specific blood, i.e., the blood of daughters of Adam. From a specific exit, which is the place of childbirth. From a specific person; as a precaution against the young girl and the menopausal woman. In a specific time, i.e., it must be in its season - it extends for a specific duration, i.e., it does not exceed ten days nor fall short of three. And it is also said in its technical interpretation: it is the blood exiting from the womb of a woman free from disease and young age.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage is a definition and ruling from the Hanafi school regarding the concept of menstruation (hayd). It begins by explaining the placement of this chapter after the earlier sections on ritual ablutions, noting that hayd and nifas (postpartum bleeding) are less frequent events. Hayd is given priority because it is more common. The definition is provided linguistically and technically. Linguistically, hayd means any blood exiting from the vulva, even from animals like a hare. Technically (sharʿan), it is restricted: (1) specific blood – only human blood; (2) specific exit – the vagina (place of childbirth); (3) specific person – not a young girl (below menstruating age) nor a menopausal woman; (4) specific time – within the menstrual age (roughly 9–55 years); (5) specific duration – between three and ten days. An alternative technical definition adds that it must be from a woman free from illness (i.e., not due to disease) and not young. The passage emphasizes the precise conditions that differentiate menstrual blood from other types of vaginal bleeding, such as istihadah (chronic bleeding), which has different rulings. This is a parsing exercise of the foundational text for memorization.

Key Terms
الحيض (al-ḥayḍ): Menstruation: blood from the womb of a woman of menstruating age within the duration of 3 to 10 days.
آيسة (āyisah): A woman who has reached menopause and no longer menstruates.
استحاضة (istiḥāḍah): Chronic non-menstrual bleeding outside the normal hayd period or beyond its limits.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Hanafi fiqh, what is the minimum and maximum duration for menstrual blood (hayd)?
A) No minimum, maximum 15 days
B) Minimum 3 days, maximum 10 days
C) Minimum 1 day, maximum 15 days
D) Minimum 3 days, maximum 7 days

Key Takeaway
Hayd is technically defined by five conditions: human blood, vaginal exit, menstruating age, duration between 3 and 10 days, and absence of disease.

The Minimum Duration of Hayd: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf's Dispute
Definition of hayd and intra-Hanafi dispute on minimum duration

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the minimum duration of hayd according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, explain the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) between their positions, and apply the mufta bihi ruling to determine hayd vs. istihadah.

Core Idea
The Hanafi minimum for hayd is three days; Abu Yusuf's view of two days and most of the third is not adopted.

This passage from al-Jawhara al-Nayira defines hayd both linguistically and legally, then dives into an intra-Hanafi dispute over its minimum duration. Understanding this dispute is crucial because it determines whether a woman's bleeding is considered hayd (requiring her to stop praying) or istihadah (allowing her to pray with wudu). We will focus on the positions of Imam Abu Hanifa and his student Abu Yusuf, and why the school ultimately relies on the three-day minimum.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Recall that Abu Hanifa holds the minimum of hayd is three days and three nights. However, note the commentator's clarification that the nights are not strictly required; three days and two nights suffice if bleeding starts before dawn.
This is the mufta bihi position.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Abu Yusuf, a senior Hanafi disciple, holds that the minimum is two days and most of the third day. He uses istihsan based on the fact that blood does not flow continuously.
Muhammad al-Shaybani's view is not mentioned here.
3
Understand the basis of the dispute
Abu Hanifa relies on a clear numerical limit to avoid uncertainty, while Abu Yusuf considers the customary experience of women. The difference is in the method: textual vs. istihsan.
This is a qiyas khafi (hidden analogy) argument.
4
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
A woman who bleeds for two days and most of the third is in hayd according to Abu Yusuf, but in istihadah according to Abu Hanifa. This affects whether she prays and fasts.
The mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الحيض

لما قدَّم ذِكْرَ الأحداث التي يكثُر وقوعها، من الأصغر والأكبر، والأحكام المتعلقة بها أصلاً وخَلَفاً : ذَكَرَ عَقيبه حكم الأحداث التي يقل وجودها، وهو الحيض والنفاس
ولهذا المعنى قَدَّمَ ذِكْرَ الحيض على النفاس ؛ لأن الحيض أكثر وقوعاً منه. والحيض في اللغة : اسمٌ لخروج الدم من الفرج على أي صفة كان، من آدمية أو غيرها، حتى قالوا: حاضت الأرنبة: إذا خرج من فرجها الدم. وفي الشرع : عبارة عن دم مخصوص، أي دم بنات آدم.
من مخرج مخصوص، وهو موضع الولادة.
من شخص مخصوص ؛ احترازاً عن الصغيرة، والآيسة.
في وقت مخصوص، وهو أن يكون في أوانه
-
يمتد مدة مخصوصة، أي لا يزيد على العشر، ولا ينقص عن الثلاث. ويقال في تفسيره شرعاً أيضاً : هو الدم الخارج من رحم امرأة سليمة
من الداء، والصغر.

أقل الحيض ثلاثة أيام ولياليها . :
وما نَقَصَ عن ذلك : فليس بحيض، وهو
استحاضة.
فقولهم : سليمة من الداء: احتراز من المستحاضة.
قال رحمه الله : (أقل الحيض: ثلاثة أيام ولياليها).
يجوز في: ثلاثة: الرفع، والنصب.
فالرفع : خبر المبتدأ، فعلى هذا لا بدَّ من إضمار تقديره: أقل مدة
الحيض؛ لأن الحيض دم لا أيام.
والنصب على الظرف.
وقوله : ولياليها لا يُشترط ثلاث ليال، بل إذا رأته ثلاثة أيام وليلتين : كان حيضاً؛ لأن العبرة للأيام، دون الليالي.
ويُحمل كلام الشيخ على ما إذا رأته في بعض النهار، فلا بد حينئذ من ثلاثة أيام وثلاث ليال؛ لأن اليوم الثالث لا يكمل إلا إلى مثله من الرابع، فيدخل ثلاث ليال، وأما لو رأته قبل طلوع الفجر، ثم طهرت عند الغروب من اليوم الثالث : كان حيضاً، وذلك ثلاثة أيام وليلتان.
- وقال أبو يوسف: أقله يومان وأكثر اليوم الثالث؛ اعتباراً للأكثر بالكل؛ لأن الأكثر من اليوم الثالث يقوم مقام كله معنى، إذ الدم لا يسيل
على الولاء.
*
قوله: (فما نقص عن ذلك : فليس بحيض، وهو استحاضة)؛ لقوله
Chapter of Hayd

When he (the author) presented the mention of the events that frequently occur, from the minor and major, and the rulings related to them originally and as a substitute, he mentioned after it the ruling of the events that rarely occur, namely hayd and nifas. And for this reason he presented the mention of hayd before nifas, because hayd occurs more frequently than it. Hayd linguistically: a name for the exit of blood from the vulva in any form it may be, from a human or other, to the extent that they said: 'the rabbit menstruates' when blood exits from its vulva. And in Shari'ah: it is an expression for a specific blood, i.e., the blood of daughters of Adam. From a specific exit, which is the place of childbirth. From a specific person, as a precaution against the young girl and the menopausal woman. In a specific time, i.e., it is in its season. Extending for a specific duration, i.e., it does not exceed ten days and does not fall short of three days. And it is said in its interpretation in Shari'ah also: it is the blood exiting from the womb of a woman free from disease and young age.

The minimum of hayd is three days and their nights. And whatever is less than that is not hayd, and it is istihadah. Their saying: free from disease is a precaution against the mustahadah (woman with irregular bleeding). He (the author), may Allah have mercy on him, said: 'The minimum of hayd is three days and their nights.' It is permissible in 'three' to have raf' or nasb. The raf' is as khabar of mubtada, so on this basis there must be an implicit word, its appreciation: 'the minimum duration of hayd'; because hayd is blood, not days. And the nasb is as a zarf (adverb). And his saying 'and their nights' does not necessitate three nights, rather if she sees it for three days and two nights, it is hayd; because consideration is for the days, not the nights. And the speech of the Shaykh is interpreted as when she sees it during part of the daytime, so then three days and three nights are necessary, because the third day is not completed except until its equivalent from the fourth, so three nights enter. But if she saw it before dawn, then she becomes pure at sunset of the third day, it is hayd, and that is three days and two nights. And Abu Yusuf said: its minimum is two days and most of the third day, considering the majority as the whole, because the majority of the third day stands in the place of the whole in meaning, since blood does not flow continuously. His saying: 'Whatever is less than that is not hayd, and it is istihadah' because of his saying...
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute from al-Jawhara al-Nayira, a commentary on al-Quduri's Mukhtasar. The author first provides linguistic and legal definitions of hayd (menstruation), emphasizing that it is blood specific to daughters of Adam, from the place of childbirth, during the reproductive years, and lasting between three and ten days. The minimum duration is then stated as three days and nights according to the relied-upon position (Abu Hanifa). The commentator explains the grammatical parsing of 'three' and clarifies that three nights are not strictly required; if bleeding begins before dawn and ends at sunset of the third day, only two nights elapse yet it is still hayd. He then reports Abu Yusuf's differing view: the minimum is two days and most of the third day, reasoning that the majority of the third day stands for the whole because blood does not flow continuously. This is an example of istihsan (juridical preference) based on custom. The passage concludes that any blood less than three days (or two days plus most of the third according to Abu Yusuf) is istihadah (irregular bleeding), which carries different legal rulings.

Key Terms
الحيض (al-hayd): Specifically, blood exiting from the womb of a woman free from disease and young age, between a minimum of three days and maximum of ten.
الاستحاضة (al-istihadah): Blood that falls short of the minimum duration of hayd (less than three days) or exceeds the maximum (ten days), considered irregular bleeding.
المستحاضة (al-mustahadah): A woman experiencing istihadah, i.e., bleeding outside the normal haid parameters.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what is the minimum duration for bleeding to be considered hayd (menstruation)?
A) One day and one night
B) Two days and most of the third day
C) Three days and three nights, but three days and two nights can suffice
D) Ten days

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi minimum for hayd is three days (mufta bihi), and bleeding less than that is istihadah, with Abu Yusuf's view being a minority within the school.

Minimum Duration of Menstruation: Three Days and Nights
Ruling on minimum menstrual period with commentary and ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi minimum for menstruation, explain the role of nights in counting, and compare the relied-upon view with Abu Yusuf's position.

Core Idea
The minimum period of menstruation in the Hanafi school is three days and nights; anything less is istihadah.

Determining when bleeding counts as menstruation (hayd) versus irregular bleeding (istihadah) is crucial for a woman's worship. This lesson unpacks al-Quduri's ruling that the minimum is three days and nights, clarifies when nights are required, and examines an intra-Hanafi disagreement by Abu Yusuf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling
Read al-Quduri's statement: 'The minimum of menstruation is three days and their nights.' This is the starting point for all discussion.
The matn is concise; the commentary expands it.
2
Understand the commentary on days vs. nights
The commentator explains that the mention of nights does not always require three full nights. If bleeding starts before dawn three days with two nights suffices. If it starts during the day, then three nights are implied because the third day extends into the next night.
This resolves potential confusion about literal interpretation.
3
Recognize the scenario for bleeding starting before dawn
The text gives an example: if bleeding begins before Fajr and ends at sunset of the third day, it counts as three days and two nights, and is hayd.
This shows practical application.
4
State Abu Yusuf's opposing view
Abu Yusuf holds that the minimum is two days and most of the third day. His reasoning is that the majority of the third day stands for the whole, since blood flow is intermittent.
This is an intra-madhhab ikhtilaf.
5
Determine the mufta bihi position
The relied-upon position is the majority view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: three full days. The commentary does not explicitly tarjih, but the matn and primary explanation support this.
The thamarah al-khilaf: a woman bleeding two days and a few hours has istihadah according to the mufta bihi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقل الحيض ثلاثة أيام ولياليها . : وما نَقَصَ عن ذلك : فليس بحيض، وهو استحاضة. فقولهم : سليمة من الداء: احتراز من المستحاضة. قال رحمه الله : (أقل الحيض: ثلاثة أيام ولياليها). يجوز في: ثلاثة: الرفع، والنصب. فالرفع : خبر المبتدأ، فعلى هذا لا بدَّ من إضمار تقديره: أقل مدة الحيض؛ لأن الحيض دم لا أيام. والنصب على الظرف. وقوله : ولياليها لا يُشترط ثلاث ليال، بل إذا رأته ثلاثة أيام وليلتين : كان حيضاً؛ لأن العبرة للأيام، دون الليالي. ويُحمل كلام الشيخ على ما إذا رأته في بعض النهار، فلا بد حينئذ من ثلاثة أيام وثلاث ليال؛ لأن اليوم الثالث لا يكمل إلا إلى مثله من الرابع، فيدخل ثلاث ليال، وأما لو رأته قبل طلوع الفجر، ثم طهرت عند الغروب من اليوم الثالث : كان حيضاً، وذلك ثلاثة أيام وليلتان. - وقال أبو يوسف: أقله يومان وأكثر اليوم الثالث؛ اعتباراً للأكثر بالكل؛ لأن الأكثر من اليوم الثالث يقوم مقام كله معنى، إذ الدم لا يسيل على الولاء. * قوله: (فما نقص عن ذلك : فليس بحيض، وهو استحاضة)؛ لقوله
The minimum of menstruation is three days and their nights. What is less than that is not menstruation; it is istihadah. Their statement: 'free from illness' is a precaution against the mustahadah. He (al-Quduri) said: 'The minimum of menstruation is three days and their nights.' It is permissible for 'three' to be nominative or accusative. The nominative is predicate of the subject, so there must be an implied [phrase] meaning 'the minimum period of menstruation,' because menstruation is blood, not days. The accusative is adverbial. His statement: 'and their nights' does not require three nights; rather if she sees it for three days and two nights, it is menstruation because the consideration is for the days, not the nights. The shaikh's statement is interpreted as when she sees it during part of the day; then three days and three nights are necessary because the third day is not complete except until its like from the fourth, so three nights enter. But if she saw it before dawn, then became pure at sunset of the third day, it is menstruation, and that is three days and two nights. Abu Yusuf said: its minimum is two days and most of the third day, considering the majority as the whole, because the majority of the third day stands in place of the whole in meaning, since blood does not flow continuously. His statement: 'What is less than that is not menstruation, it is istihadah' due to his statement [the Prophet's].
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of al-Quduri's matn on the minimum duration of menstruation. The ruling states that the minimum is three full days and nights; anything less is istihadah (irregular bleeding). The commentary clarifies grammatical variants and explains that the nights are not strictly required: if blood is seen for three days but only two nights, it is still menstruation. However, if the bleeding starts during the day, then three full days and three nights are needed because the third day carries over. The commentator addresses a scenario: if bleeding begins before dawn and ends at sunset of the third day, it counts as three days and two nights. Then Abu Yusuf's dissenting view is presented: he holds that two days plus most of the third suffice, analogizing the majority of the day to the whole due to the intermittent nature of blood flow. The passage ends with a reference to the prophetic hadith, which is the primary proof, though not fully quoted here.

Key Terms
حيض (hayd): The natural monthly bleeding of a woman of menstrual age, with minimum and maximum durations as specified.
استحاضة (istihadah): Irregular, non-menstrual bleeding that does not meet the criteria of hayd; it does not prevent prayer or fasting.
الكدرة (al-kudrah): Muddy or discolored discharge that may or may not be considered menstruation depending on context.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi view, what is the minimum duration of menstrual bleeding?
A) One day
B) Two days and most of the third
C) Three days and nights
D) Two days and two nights

Key Takeaway
The minimum menstruation period in the Hanafi school is three full days and nights; any bleeding less than that is istihadah, and the woman must pray and fast as usual.
