Definition of al-Mughawwisah
Defines the legal term 'al-mughawwisah'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of al-mughawwisah and identify its legal implication (prohibition of deception) in Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
Al-mughawwisah: a woman's false claim of menstruation to avoid marital intimacy is a prohibited deception.

In Hanafi fiqh, specific terms are used for distinct forms of marital deception. The term al-mughawwisah captures a particular scenario where a wife falsely alleges menstruation to avoid sexual relations. This definition, though brief, carries an immediate legal ruling: the falsehood is sinful. Understanding the precise meaning helps you recognize the boundaries of permissible and impermissible conduct in marriage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject
First, note the term being defined: 'al-mughawwisah'. It is a noun describing a type of woman.
The root 'gh-w-s' implies deceit.
2
Parse the definition's components
Break down the definition into: (1) who? a woman, (2) what does she do? says to her husband 'I am menstruating', (3) what is the reality? she is actually pure (not menstruating), (4) why? to prevent intercourse.
The contrast between claim and reality is crucial.
3
Recognize the legal implication
Understand that this definition implicitly rules the act as prohibited (haram) because it involves lying and deception, both of which are forbidden in marriage.
The term itself warns against this deception.
4
Distinguish from similar cases
Differentiate al-mughawwisah from a woman who genuinely has irregular menstruation (istihadah) or who truthfully states her condition. The key is the falsehood.
Only false claim for avoidance qualifies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمغوصةُ: التي تقول لزوجها : إنها حائض، وهي طاهرة؛ حتى لا يجامعها.
And al-mughawwisah: she who says to her husband: 'I am menstruating,' while she is pure, so that he does not have intercourse with her.
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage defines a legal term: al-mughawwisah. The term refers to a woman who falsely claims to be menstruating to avoid sexual relations with her husband while she is actually in a state of purity (not menstruating). This is a form of deception and is prohibited. The ruling is implicitly that such falsehood is sinful, but the text only provides the definition.

Key Terms
المغوصة (al-mughawwisah): A woman who falsely claims to be menstruating to deter her husband from intercourse.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the defining characteristic of al-mughawwisah?
A) She has prolonged menstruation.
B) She says she is menstruating while she is actually pure to avoid intercourse.
C) She experiences spotting between periods.
D) She is a woman in nifas.

Key Takeaway
False claim of menstruation to avoid marital intimacy is a prohibited act of deception known as al-mughawwisah in Hanafi fiqh.

Prohibition of Qur'an Recitation for Junub and Ha'id: The Less-Than-Ayah Dispute
Commentary on the prohibition ruling with proof and an intra-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on Qur'an recitation for a menstruating woman and junub, explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding less than an ayah, and apply the exception for unintentional dhikr.

Core Idea
Reciting even less than an ayah is prohibited, except when not intended as recitation — the ruling depends on intention.

In this passage, the commentator unpacks a core ruling from the matn: the prohibition of Qur'an recitation for those in major ritual impurity (junub) or menstrual bleeding (ha'id). He provides both a hadith proof and an analogy, then addresses an intra-Hanafi dispute about the scope of the prohibition. The key nuance is that not all utterances of Qur'anic words are considered recitation — if the intention is general dhikr, the prohibition lifts. This lesson will guide you through the ruling, its evidence, the dispute, and the practical implication of intention.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its primary dalil
The matn states it is not permissible (la yajuz). The commentator cites a hadith: 'The junub and menstruating woman should not recite anything from the Qur'an.' Note the words 'shay'an min al-Qur'an' (anything from the Qur'an).
2
Understand the qiyas (analogy)
The commentator adds an analogy: recitation involves the mouth and tongue, which are impure (require washing), so handling the Qur'an with an impure body part is not allowed. This reinforces the prohibition.
3
Locate the intra-Hanafi dispute
The phrase 'wa hadha' indicates that the apparent meaning treats an ayah and less than an ayah equally in prohibition. Then al-Tahawi is cited as permitting less than an ayah. The author prefers the majority view ('al-awwal asahh').
Al-Tahawi is a Hanafi scholar; his view is a minority within the school.
4
Analyze the exception for intention
The commentators add an exception: if the words are not intended as Qur'anic recitation but as dhikr (e.g., 'Alhamdulillah' for thanks, 'Bismillah' before eating), they are permissible. The key is the intention (niyyah).
This exception is based on the principle that actions are judged by intentions.
5
Apply the ruling to real scenarios
Practice distinguishing: a menstruating woman reciting Ayat al-Kursi (whole ayah) is haram; reciting 'Bismillah' before eating with the intention of recitation is haram, but with intention of dhikr is halal. State the mufta bihi (full prohibition for less than an ayah).
The mufta bihi is the majority view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ولا يجوز لحائض ولا جُنُب قراءة القرآن).
لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام : «لا يقرأ الجُنُب ولا الحائض شيئاً من القرآن (۱).
ولأنه يباشر القرآن بعضو يجب غسله، فلا يجوز. وكذا لا تجوز له القراءة حالة الوطء.
- والنفساء: كالحائض.
وظاهر هذا أن الآية وما دونها: سواء في التحريم.
وقال الطحاوي: يجوز لهم ما دون الآية، والأول أصح.
قالوا: إلا أن لا يقصد بما دون الآية القراءة، مثل أن يقول: الحمد الله : يريد الشكر، أو: الله : : عند الأكل، أو غيره، فإنه لا بأس به؛ لأنهما لا يمنعان من ذكر الله.
His statement: (It is not permissible for a menstruating woman nor a junub person to recite the Qur'an). Due to his saying, peace be upon him: 'The junub and the menstruating woman should not recite anything from the Qur'an.' And because he handles the Qur'an with a body part that must be washed, so it is not permissible. Likewise, recitation is not permissible during intercourse. And the postpartum woman is like the menstruating woman. The apparent meaning is that an ayah and less than an ayah are the same in prohibition. Al-Tahawi said: It is permissible for them [to recite] less than an ayah, but the first [opinion] is more correct. They said: Except if by less than an ayah they do not intend recitation, such as saying 'Alhamdulillah' meaning gratitude, or 'Bismillah' during eating or other things, then there is no harm because these do not prevent [them] from remembering Allah.
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn ruling prohibiting Qur'an recitation for a menstruating woman and a junub (person in a state of major ritual impurity). The author provides two proofs: first, a hadith explicitly stating that neither the junub nor the menstruating woman should recite anything from the Qur'an; second, an analogy that the person is handling the Qur'an with a body part that requires washing (i.e., the mouth and tongue are impure), so it is not allowed. The same applies during intercourse. The postpartum woman (nifas) is given the same ruling as the menstruating woman. Then there is an intra-Hanafi dispute: the majority holds that even a short verse (less than an ayah) is prohibited, while al-Tahawi permits it. The author prefers the majority view. However, an exception is made if the words are not intended as Qur'anic recitation but as general dhikr, like saying 'Alhamdulillah' out of gratitude or 'Bismillah' before eating. Such utterances are not prohibited because they fall under the category of remembering Allah, which is permissible for everyone.

Key Terms
جُنُب (junub): A person in a state of major ritual impurity (janabah) requiring ghusl, typically due to sexual intercourse or emission of semen.
حائض (ha'id): A woman experiencing menstrual bleeding (hayd), which renders her ritually impure.
قراءة القرآن (qira'at al-Qur'an): Reciting the Qur'an with intention of tilawah (recitation).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the ruling if a menstruating woman says 'Alhamdulillah' as an expression of gratitude?
A) This is prohibited because it is part of the Qur'an.
B) This is permissible because she does not intend Qur'anic recitation.
C) This is makruh tanzih.
D) This is wajib.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school prohibits any part of the Qur'an for a menstruating woman or junub when recited with the intention of tilawah, but permits words from the Qur'an when said as general dhikr.

Prohibition of Quran Recitation for Junub, Ha'id, and Nufasa'
Establishing the ruling on recitation and exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on Quran recitation for the junub and menstruating woman, identify the proof, and distinguish the exception for general dhikr.

Core Idea
A junub or ha'id is prohibited from reciting any Quran, but may engage in general dhikr like tasbih and tahlil.

This passage from al-Quduri's Mukhtasar establishes the prohibition of Quran recitation for those in a state of major impurity. The ruling is based on a hadith and a rational analogy. You will learn the relied-upon Hanafi view and the exception for unintentional dhikr.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its subject
Determine who is prohibited (junub, ha'id, nufasa') and what act is prohibited (reciting Quran).
The prohibition applies equally to all three categories.
2
Examine the hadith proof
The text cites the hadith: 'لا يقرأ الجُنُب ولا الحائض شيئاً من القرآن'. Analyze its explicit prohibition.
The word 'شيئاً' (anything) indicates total prohibition.
3
Understand the qiyas (rational argument)
Recitation involves the mouth, an organ that must be washed in ghusl; analogically, it is not allowed.
This qiyas reinforces the hadith ruling.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi difference
Al-Tahawi permitted less than a verse; the relied-upon view prohibits all amounts. Al-Quduri prefers the first view.
The mufta bihi is the prohibition of any amount.
5
Distinguish the exception for dhikr
If a person says a Quranic phrase without intending recitation (e.g., 'Alhamdulillah' as thanks), it is allowed.
General dhikr like tasbih and tahlil is explicitly permitted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الحيض
لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام : «لا يقرأ الجُنُب ولا الحائض شيئاً من القرآن».
ولأنه يباشر القرآن بعضو يجب غسله، فلا يجوز. وكذا لا تجوز له القراءة حالة الوطء.
- والنفساء: كالحائض.
وظاهر هذا أن الآية وما دونها: سواء في التحريم.
وقال الطحاوي: يجوز لهم ما دون الآية، والأول أصح.
قالوا: إلا أن لا يقصد بما دون الآية القراءة، مثل أن يقول: الحمد لله : يريد الشكر، أو: الله : : عند الأكل، أو غيره، فإنه لا بأس به؛ لأنهما لا يمنعان من ذكر الله.
ولا بأس للجنب والحائض والنفساء أن يسبحوا الله ، ويهللوه.
Chapter of Menstruation. Due to his saying, peace be upon him: 'The junub and the menstruating woman should not recite anything of the Quran.' And because he directly handles the Quran with an organ that must be washed, it is not permissible. Similarly, recitation is not permissible for him during intercourse. And the postpartum woman is like the menstruating woman. The apparent meaning of this is that a verse and what is less than it are equal in prohibition. Al-Tahawi said: It is permissible for them (to recite) what is less than a verse, and the first view is more correct. They said: Except if they do not intend recitation by what is less than a verse, such as saying 'Alhamdulillah' intending thanks, or 'Allah' at the time of eating, or other than that, then there is no harm in it, because they are not prevented from the remembrance of Allah. And there is no harm for the junub, menstruating woman, and postpartum woman to glorify Allah and declare His oneness.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This is a ruling with proof establishing that a person in a state of major impurity (junub or ha'id or nufasa') is prohibited from reciting the Quran. The proof is the hadith stating they should not recite anything. The commentator adds a rational argument: reciting involves the mouth, which is an organ that must be washed in ghusl, so it is not allowed. The prohibition applies to any amount of Quran, whether a full verse or less, according to the preferred view; al-Tahawi's opinion that less than a verse is allowed is considered weaker. However, if the person does not intend recitation but rather general dhikr (like saying 'Alhamdulillah' as thanks), it is permissible because dhikr is allowed. At the end, tasbih and tahlil are explicitly permitted. The same rulings apply to the postpartum woman (nufasa') as to the menstruating woman.

Key Terms
الجُنُب (al-junub): A person in a state of major ritual impurity requiring ghusl due to sexual intercourse or emission.
الحائض (al-ha'id): A menstruating woman, who is in a state of major impurity similar to junub for the duration of her period.
ما دون الآية (ma duna al-ayah): Less than a full verse of the Quran, such as a part of a verse or a single word.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi view, what is the ruling on a menstruating woman reciting a single verse of the Quran?
A) It is permissible.
B) It is prohibited.
C) It is makruh.
D) It is wajib.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school prohibits all Quran recitation for those in major impurity, but permits general dhikr, with the relied-upon view rejecting any exception for less than a verse.

Writing the Quran in a State of Major Impurity: An Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on writing Quran for junub/ha'id

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi opinions on writing the Quran for a junub/ha'id and apply the distinction between direct and indirect contact to determine the ruling.

Core Idea
Writing the Quran while junub is not absolutely prohibited — it depends on direct contact with the written word.

The permissibility of writing the Quran for a person in major impurity (junub or ha'id) is a nuanced issue within the Hanafi school. Two distinct opinions are recorded: one strict prohibition and another that distinguishes between direct and indirect contact. Understanding this difference is key to applying the correct ruling and appreciating how later Hanafi scholars refined the school's position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Opinions
Locate the two cited sources: 'Umniat al-Musalli' holds absolute prohibition (la yajuz); 'Al-Khujandi' holds it is makruh only when the person directly touches the writing surface.
Both scholars are Hanafi, showing internal diversity.
2
Pinpoint the Key Condition
Focus on the phrase 'if they are in direct contact with the tablet and paper' (إذا كان مباشراً للوح والبياض). This is the condition that triggers makruh according to Al-Khujandi.
Direct contact means the hand touches the paper or tablet directly.
3
Analyze the Concession
The text adds: if the writing implement is placed on the ground and the hand does not touch the written words, there is no harm. This shows that the prohibition is linked to touching the words, not the act of writing itself.
This concession aligns with the ruling on touching the Mushaf: a junub may not touch the Mushaf but may carry it with a barrier.
4
Determine the Mufta Bihi
Although the text does not explicitly choose, the inclusion of the concession suggests that the operative view is the makruh position (indirect writing is permissible). The absolute prohibition is weaker.
Later fatwa works generally adopt the makruh view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهل يجوز للجنب والحائض كتابة القرآن؟
قال في «أمنية المصلي» : لا يجوز.
وفي «الخُجندي»: يكره للجنب والحائض كتابة القرآن إذا كان مباشراً للوح والبياض.
- وإن وضعهما على الأرض، وكتبه غير أن يضع يده على المكتوب: لا بأس به.
And is it permissible for the junub and the menstruating woman to write the Quran? It is said in 'Umniat al-Musalli': It is not permissible. And in 'Al-Khujandi': It is disliked (makruh) for the junub and menstruating woman to write the Quran if they are in direct contact with the tablet and the paper. And if they place them on the ground and write without placing their hand on the written (words), there is no harm in it.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding writing the Quran for those in major impurity. Two views are cited: the first from 'Umniat al-Musalli' states it is absolutely impermissible. The second from 'Al-Khujandi' holds it is makruh (disliked) only if the person directly touches the tablet and paper. The text then adds a concession: if the writing implement is placed on the ground and the hand does not touch the written words, there is no harm. This indicates that the prohibition is connected to direct contact with the written word, similar to the ruling on touching the Mushaf.

Key Terms
البیاض (al-bayad): The white paper or writing surface.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Only if writing on a Mushaf.

Key Takeaway
The permissibility of writing Quran for a junub/ha'id depends on direct contact with the written words; indirect writing is permissible.

Touching Mushaf, Tahajji, and Teaching for Ha'id
Ruling with proof using a fortiori analogy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on touching the Mushaf for a muhdith, explain the a fortiori extension to major impurity, and apply the exception for tahajji and teaching word-by-word.

Core Idea
Major impurity bars both hand and mouth; touching Mushaf is even more restricted than reciting.

This passage tackles a nuanced area of taharah: what acts are allowed for those in impurity regarding the Quran. You already know that a muhdith cannot touch the Mushaf without a cover. Now we extend that rule to junub, ha'id, and nufasa' using a rational proof, and explore exceptions like spelling out letters and teaching children word-by-word.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish tahajji from recitation
Identify why spelling out (tahajji) is permissible for those in major impurity — it is not considered qira'ah because the letters are not combined into meaningful verses.
Tahajji includes saying 'Alif, Lam, Meem'.
2
Understand the exception for teaching
Note that later Hanafi scholars allowed ha'id/nufasa' teachers to dictate Quran word-by-word due to necessity (they cannot perform ghusl). A junub cannot do this because he can remove impurity.
The teacher must pause between words, not recite complete verses.
3
Trace the a fortiori argument
The matn only prohibits touching for muhdith, but the commentator explains this applies more strongly to major impurity. Reason: recitation is lighter than touching; if recitation is prohibited for them, touching is even more prohibited.
This is a qiyas awlawi (a fortiori analogy).
4
Examine the proof via the extent of impurity
The difference lies in which body parts are implicated: minor impurity only affects the hands (washing is fard), while major impurity affects both hands and mouth (both are fard in ghusl). Hence the prohibition on touching is stronger for major impurity.
The illa is the presence of impurity on the touching organ.
5
Define the acceptable cover (ghilaf)
The cover must be separate from the Mushaf (e.g., a bag or pouch), not attached like the leather binding. This ensures a barrier between the impurity and the sacred text.
The condition 'muta'afi 'anhu' — something that stands apart.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما التهجي بالقرآن فلا بأس به.
وقال بعض المتأخرين : إذا كانت الحائض أو النفساء معلّمة: جاز لها أن تلقن الصبيان كلمة كلمةً، وتقطع بين الكلمتين، ولا تلقنهم آية كاملة ؛ لأنها مضطرة إلى التعليم، وهي لا تقدر على رفع حدثها.
فعلى هذا لا يجوز للجنب ذلك ؛ لأنه يقدر على رفع حدثه. ولا بأس للجنب والحائض والنفساء أن يسبحوا الله ، ويهللوه.
قوله: (ولا يجوز لمحدث مس المصحف).
وإنما لم يذكر الحائض والنفساء والجنب؛ لأنه يُعلم أن حكمهم حكمه بطريق الأولى؛ لأن حكم القراءة أخفُ من حكم المس، فإذا لم تجز لهم القراءة: فلأن لا يجوز لهم المس أولى.
والفرق في المحدث بين المس والقراءة: أن الحدث حل اليد، دون الفم، والجنابة حلت اليد والفم، ألا ترى أن غسل اليد والفم في الجنابة فرضان، وفي الحدث إنما يُفرض غسل اليد، دون الفم.
* قوله : (إلا أن يأخذه بغلافه، أو بعلاقته).
وغلافه: ما يكون متجافياً عنه، أي متباعداً، بأن يكون شيئاً ثالثاً بين الماس والممسوس، كالجراب والخريطة، دون ما هو متصل به، كالجلد
As for spelling out (tahajji) the Quran, there is no harm in it. And some later scholars said: If the menstruating woman or postpartum woman is a teacher, it is permissible for her to dictate to children word by word, and pause between the two words, and not dictate a complete verse, because she is compelled to teach and cannot remove her impurity. Based on this, it is not permissible for the junub to do that, because he can remove his impurity. And there is no harm for the junub, menstruating woman, and postpartum woman to glorify Allah and declare His oneness. His statement: (And it is not permissible for a muhdith to touch the Mushaf). And he did not mention the menstruating woman, postpartum woman, and junub because it is known that their ruling is the same as his a fortiori; because the ruling of recitation is lighter than the ruling of touching, so if recitation is not permissible for them, then touching is even more impermissible. The difference between the muhdith regarding touching and recitation is that minor impurity only involves the hand, not the mouth, while major impurity involves both hand and mouth. Do you not see that washing the hand and mouth in janabah are two obligations, while in minor impurity only washing the hand is obligatory, not the mouth. His statement: (except that he takes it with its cover or its strap). And its cover is something that is separate from it, i.e., distant, such that there is a third thing between the toucher and the touched, like a bag or a pouch, not something attached to it like the skin (of the book).
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage covers several rulings. First, spelling out (tahajji) the Quran letter by letter is permissible for those in major impurity. Second, a later opinion allows a menstruating or postpartum teacher to teach children word by word (not complete verses) due to necessity, but a junub cannot because he can perform ghusl. Third, tasbih and tahlil are always allowed. Then the commentator explains why the original matn only mentioned muhdith for touching Mushaf: the ruling for major impurity is a fortiori stronger. The proof is a rational argument: minor impurity only affects the hands, while major impurity affects both hands and mouth, as seen in the fard components of ghusl versus wudu. Finally, the cover must be a separate object like a bag or pouch, not something attached like leather binding.

Key Terms
التهجي (al-tahajji): Spelling out the letters of the Quran, such as 'Alif, Lam, Meem'.
الغلاف (al-ghilaf): The cover of the Mushaf, which must be separate (muta'afi 'anhu) from the book itself, like a bag or pouch.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Teaching Quran word-by-word
C) Spelling out the letters of the Quran
D) Touching the Mushaf with a separate cover

Key Takeaway
Touching Mushaf without a separate cover is haram for all in impurity, but tahajji and dhikr are always allowed; teaching word-by-word is a concession for ha'id/nufasa' only.

Touching the Mushaf and the Three Types of Hadath
Commentary on prohibition of touching Mushaf with exceptions and classification of impurities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on touching the Mushaf for each type of hadath, identify the conditions for permissible touch, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on the definition of ghilaf.

Core Idea
Touching the Mushaf is haram without a separate barrier; the prohibition is graded by impurity type.

This lesson unpacks the Hanafi ruling on touching the Mushaf (the physical copy of the Qur'an) while in a state of ritual impurity. The passage from Al-Jawhara al-Nayyira clarifies the absolute prohibition for one with hadath, the exception for using a wrapper (ghilaf), and the classification of impurities into minor, medium, and major. It also addresses the practical case of a menstruating teacher and the intra-Hanafi difference regarding what constitutes a valid barrier.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what act is being ruled upon (touching the Mushaf) and the hukm level (haram). Note that the text uses 'la yajuz' implying prohibition.
The prohibition applies to anyone in a state of hadath, but the text focuses on minor hadath first.
2
Identify the exception and its condition
The exception is taking the Mushaf with its wrapper (ghilaf). The condition is that the wrapper must be separate (ghilaf muttajaf) from the Mushaf.
The text distinguishes between attached and separate wrappers.
3
Understand the a fortiori reasoning for janaba, hayd, and nifas
The author explains that if recitation is prohibited for these groups (which is lighter), then touching is even more prohibited. This is based on the logic that hadath affects the hand, while janaba affects both hand and mouth.
The term 'hadath' in the base text is inclusive of all impurities, but the commentary clarifies the gradation.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on ghilaf
Contrast the correct view (ghilaf must be separate) with al-Isbijabi's view (attached leather suffices). Identify the evidence: attached leather is tabi' (follower) of the Mushaf, so touching it is like touching the Mushaf itself.
The fatwa is on the first view, as stated by the author.
5
Apply the ruling to related cases
Extend the prohibition to touching paper with Quranic verses, coins, and tafsir books at the Quranic portions. Distinguish from other parts of tafsir books which can be touched.
All these items are considered tabi' (followers) of the Mushaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الحيض
ولا يجوز لمُحْدِث مس المصحف إلا أن يأخذه بغلافه .
- وإن وضعهما على الأرض، وكتبه غير أن يضع يده على المكتوب: لا بأس به.
وأما التهجي بالقرآن فلا بأس به.
وقال بعض المتأخرين : إذا كانت الحائض أو النفساء معلّمة: جاز لها أن تلقن الصبيان كلمة كلمةً، وتقطع بين الكلمتين، ولا تلقنهم آية كاملة ؛ لأنها مضطرة إلى التعليم، وهي لا تقدر على رفع حدثها. فعلى هذا لا يجوز للجنب ذلك ؛ لأنه يقدر على رفع حدثه. ولا بأس للجنب والحائض والنفساء أن يسبحوا الله ، ويهللوه. قوله: (ولا يجوز لمحدث مس المصحف). وإنما لم يذكر الحائض والنفساء والجنب؛ لأنه يُعلم أن حكمهم حكمه بطريق الأولى؛ لأن حكم القراءة أخفُ من حكم المس، فإذا لم تجز لهم القراءة: فلأن لا يجوز لهم المس أولى. والفرق في المحدث بين المس والقراءة: أن الحدث حل اليد، دون الفم، والجنابة حلّت اليد والفم، ألا ترى أن غسل اليد والفم في الجنابة فرضان، وفي الحدث إنما يُفرض غسل اليد، دون الفم. * قوله : (إلا أن يأخذه بغلافه، أو بعلاقته). وغلافه: ما يكون متجافياً عنه، أي متباعداً، بأن يكون شيئاً ثالثاً بين الماس والممسوس، كالجراب والخريطة، دون ما هو متصل به، كالجلد المشرز، هو الصحيح. وعند الإسبيجابي: الغلاف هو : الجلد المتصل به، والصحيح الأول، وعليه الفتوى؛ لأن الجلد تبع للمصحف. وإذا لم يجز للمحدث المس: فكذا لا يجوز له وضع أصابعه على الورق المكتوب فيه عند التقليب؛ لأنه تبع له. وكذا لا يجوز له مس شيء مكتوب فيه شيء من القرآن، من لوح أو درهم أو غير ذلك إذا كان آيةً تامة. وكذا كتب التفسير لا يجوز مس موضع القرآن منها، وله أن يمس غيره، بخلاف المصحف؛ لأن جميع ذلك تبع له. وحاصله : أن الأحداث ثلاثة حدث صغير، وحدث وسط، وحدث كبير: -١- فالصغير ما يوجب الوضوء، لا غير كالبول، والغائط، والقيء إذا ملأ الفم، وخروج الدم والقيح من البدن إذا تجاوز إلى موضع يلحقه حكم التطهير. والحدث الوسط هو: الجنابة. والحدث الكبير : هو الحيض والنفاس. فتأثير الحدثِ الصغير: تحريم الصلاة، وسجدة التلاوة، ومس المصحف، وكراهة الطواف.
Chapter of Menstruation. It is not permissible for one who has a minor impurity (hadath) to touch the Mushaf unless he takes it with its wrapper. - If he places them on the ground and writes it, except that he places his hand on the written (text), there is no harm. As for spelling out (the letters of) the Qur'an, there is no harm in it. Some later scholars said: If a menstruating or postpartum woman is a teacher, it is permissible for her to dictate to children word by word, and she pauses between the two words, and does not dictate a complete verse; because she is compelled by teaching, and she is unable to remove her impurity. Based on this, it is not permissible for a junub to do that, because he is able to remove his impurity. There is no harm for the junub, menstruating, and postpartum woman to glorify Allah and declare His oneness. His statement: (It is not permissible for one who has hadath to touch the Mushaf). He did not mention the menstruating, postpartum, and junub because it is known that their ruling is the same by a fortiori reasoning; because the ruling of recitation is lighter than the ruling of touching. So if recitation is not permissible for them, then it is even more so that touching is not permissible. The difference regarding the hadath between touching and recitation is that hadath only affects the hand, not the mouth, while janaba affects both the hand and mouth. Do you not see that washing the hand and mouth in janaba are two obligatory acts, while in hadath only washing the hand is obligatory. His statement: (Unless he takes it with its wrapper or its strap). Its wrapper (ghilaf) is that which is separated from it, i.e., distant, such that there is a third thing between the toucher and the touched, like a bag or a pouch, not that which is attached to it, like the leather cover (with stitching). That is the correct view. According to al-Isbijabi, the wrapper is the attached leather, but the first is correct, and the fatwa is upon it, because the leather is a follower of the Mushaf. If it is not permissible for a person with hadath to touch, likewise it is not permissible for him to place his fingers on the paper in which it is written when turning pages, because it is a follower of it. Similarly, it is not permissible for him to touch anything on which something of the Qur'an is written, such as a slate or a dirham or other than that, if it is a complete verse. Similarly, books of tafsir: it is not permissible to touch the place of the Qur'an from them, but he may touch other than that, unlike the Mushaf, because all of that is a follower of it. Summary: The ahdath are three: minor hadath, medium hadath, and major hadath. 1. The minor is that which necessitates wudu only, like urine, feces, vomit if it fills the mouth, and the exit of blood and pus from the body if it spreads to an area subject to the ruling of purification. The medium hadath is janaba. The major hadath is menstruation and postpartum bleeding. The effect of minor hadath: prohibition of prayer, prostration of recitation, touching the Mushaf, and dislike of circumambulation.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the chapter of menstruation from Mukhtasar al-Quduri. It begins by stating the ruling that one who is in a state of hadath (minor impurity) is not permitted to touch the Mushaf directly; he may only do so with a wrapper (ghilaf) that is separate from the Mushaf. The author then notes exceptions: placing a Mushaf on the ground while writing from it (without directly touching the written text) is allowed, as is spelling out the letters of the Quran. The text then addresses a fatwa from later scholars regarding a menstruating or postpartum woman who is a teacher: she may teach children word by word because necessity overrides the prohibition, but a person in janaba (major impurity) may not do so because he can remove his impurity. Following this, the commentary explains why the original ruling only mentioned 'hadath' and not janaba, hayd, or nifas: because these are more severe, and the prohibition on recitation (which is lighter) implies the prohibition on touching. The comment clarifies the difference between hadath and janaba: hadath only affects the hands, while janaba affects both hands and mouth, requiring washing of both in ghusl. Regarding the wrapper, the correct view is that it must be a separate barrier like a bag or pouch, not attached leather (ghilaf muttasil). The commentary then extends the prohibition to touching paper with Quranic writing, slates, dirhams with verses, and tafsir books at the Quranic portion. Finally, it summarizes the three levels of impurity: minor (requiring wudu), medium (janaba), and major (hayd/nifas), listing the effects of minor hadath: prohibition of prayer, sajda tilawa, touching the Mushaf, and dislike of tawaf.

Key Terms
حدث (hadath): A state of ritual impurity that prevents certain acts of worship. In this passage, three levels are distinguished: minor (صغير), medium (وسط), and major (كبير).
غلاف (ghilaf): A cover or wrapper that provides a barrier between the hand and the Mushaf. The text specifies it must be separate (متجاف) from the Mushaf, not attached like a leather binding.
تباع (tabi'): A follower or attached component. Here, the attached leather cover is considered tabi' to the Mushaf, so touching it is like touching the Mushaf itself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the ruling for a menstruating woman who is a teacher of the Quran?
A) She may teach complete verses without restriction.
B) She may teach word by word but not a complete verse.
C) She may not teach at all until she becomes pure.
D) She may teach only by writing, not by reciting.

Key Takeaway
Touching the Mushaf is prohibited for anyone with any form of hadath, with the only exception being the use of a separate barrier (ghilaf), and the prohibition is stricter for major impurities.

Definitions of Medium and Major Impurity and Ruling on Looking at the Quran
Defining medium and major impurity and ruling on looking at Quran for junub

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the legal effects of medium and major impurity and explain the Hanafi ruling on looking at the Quran for a junub.

Core Idea
Looking at the Quran is permissible for a junub; impurity does not affect the eye.

This passage from Sharh al-Jawhara al-Nayira clarifies the two categories of ritual impurity in Hanafi fiqh and their distinctive prohibitions. It then addresses the specific question of whether a person in a state of major impurity may look at the Quran, resolving a potential misunderstanding about the scope of impurity's effect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two levels of hadath
Locate the terms 'al-hadath al-awsat' (medium impurity) and 'al-hadath al-kabir' (major impurity) in the text. Note that medium impurity requires wudu, while major impurity requires ghusl.
These terms are specific to Hanafi fiqh.
2
List the prohibitions unique to each level
Extract the additional prohibitions beyond the common ones for medium impurity (reciting Quran, entering mosque) and major impurity (fasting, intercourse, and divorce dislike). Understand the hierarchy of restrictions.
The common prohibitions (prayer, tawaf, touching mushaf) apply to both.
3
Understand the rationale for looking at the Quran
The text states that looking is not disliked because impurity does not affect the eye. Identify the analogy: water is not required to reach the eye in purification, so impurity does not affect sight.
This is a qiyas (analogical reasoning) basis.
4
Address the objection about partial purification
A hypothetical objection argues that rinsing the mouth (for junub) or washing hands (for minor impurity) should allow recitation or touching. The text rejects this, affirming that the state of impurity is only removed by full purification (ghusl or wudu).
The correct view is from al-Idah al-Sayrafi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحدث الأوسط تأثيره: تحريم هذه الأشياء المذكورة، ويزيد عليها بتحريم قراءة القرآن، ودخول المسجد. والحدث الكبير تأثيره: تحريم هذه الأشياء كلها، ويزيد عليها بتحريم الصوم، وتحريم الوطء، وكراهة الطلاق. *ولا يكره للجنب والحائض والنفساء النظرُ إلى المصحف؛ لأن الجنابة لا تحل العين، ألا ترى أنه لا يُفرض إيصال الماء إليها. فإن قلت: فلو تمضمض الجنب فقد ارتفع حدث الفم، فينبغي أن تجوز له التلاوة، فهل هو كذلك؟ قال بعضهم: يجوز، والصحيح أنه لا يجوز؛ لأنه بذلك لا ترتفع جنابته. : - وكذا إذا غَسَلَ المحدث يديه هل يجوز له المس؟ الصحيح أنه لا يجوز؛ لما قلنا كذا في إيضاح الصيرفي.
The effect of medium impurity: prohibition of these mentioned things, and added to them prohibition of reciting Quran and entering the mosque. The effect of major impurity: prohibition of all these things, and added to them prohibition of fasting, prohibition of intercourse, and dislike of divorce. *It is not disliked for the junub, menstruating woman, and postpartum woman to look at the Quran; because janaba does not vitiate the eye, do you not see that it is not obligatory to deliver water to it? If you say: if the junub rinses his mouth, then the impurity of the mouth has been removed, so it should be permissible for him to recite? Is it so? Some said: it is permissible, and the correct view is that it is not permissible; because by that his janaba is not removed. - Likewise, if the person with minor impurity washes his hands, is it permissible for him to touch? The correct view is that it is not permissible; for what we said, as in 'Idah al-Sayrafi.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage from Sharh al-Jawhara al-Nayira defines two categories of ritual impurity (hadath) and their legal effects. The 'medium impurity' (hadath aswad) refers to minor impurity (like breaking wudu), which prohibits prayer, circumambulation, touching the Quran, etc. The 'major impurity' (hadath kabir) includes janaba, menstruation, and postpartum bleeding, which also prohibits recitation, fasting, and intercourse, and makes divorce disliked. The passage then clarifies that looking at the Quran without touching is permissible for those in major impurity, because impurity does not affect the eyes. It then addresses an objection: if a junub rinses his mouth (with the intention of purification), the mouth becomes pure, so why can't he recite? The answer is that partial purification does not remove janaba; he remains junub until he performs ghusl. Similarly, washing hands for a minor impurity does not permit touching the Quran. This is the correct view according to al-Sayrafi.

Key Terms
الحدث الأوسط (al-hadath al-awsat): Medium impurity, referring to minor ritual impurity (i.e., breaking wudu) that requires wudu
الحدث الكبير (al-hadath al-kabīr): Major impurity (janaba, hayd, nifas) requiring ghusl
جنب (junub): Person in state of major impurity after sexual intercourse or ejaculation

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, is it permissible for a junub to look at the Quran?
A) No, it is prohibited
B) Yes, it is permissible
C) It is disliked
D) It is recommended

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, major impurity prohibits recitation and touching but not looking at the Quran; partial purification does not lift these prohibitions.

Ruling on Intercourse When Menstrual Blood Stops Before Ten Days
Ruling with proof on timing of permissible intercourse after menstruation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on when intercourse becomes permissible after menstruation ends and apply the distinction between stopping at the habitual time versus before it.

Core Idea
Intercourse is not permitted after blood stops until ghusl or a full prayer time passes; if blood stops before the habitual duration, wait until the habit completes.

A common question in fiqh of menstruation is when a woman may resume marital relations after her period ends. The Hanafi school provides a detailed ruling that depends on whether the bleeding stops at her usual habit or earlier. This passage from Sharh al-Jawhara explains the matn of al-Quduri and adds precautionary measures from later sources. By understanding the distinction, you will grasp the Hanafi approach to avoiding impurity through ihtiyat.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage addresses intercourse with a menstruating woman whose blood stops before the maximum of ten days. The ruling is that it is not permissible until one of two conditions is met.
The act is intercourse; the hukm is haram unless the condition is fulfilled.
2
Understand the conditions for permissibility
Two conditions are given: either the woman performs ghusl (ritual bath) or a complete prayer time passes over her. 'Complete' means a time when a full fard prayer can be offered, like Dhuhr, not incomplete times like Duha or Eid.
The phrase 'waqt salat kamil' is defined precisely to avoid ambiguity.
3
Distinguish between stopping at habit vs. before habit
If blood stops exactly at her habitual number of days, the conditions above apply. If it stops before her habit, even if she performs ghusl, she must wait until her usual habit time passes before intercourse is allowed.
This is based on the likelihood of blood returning during the habit period.
4
Apply the precautionary principle (ihtiyat)
The ruling emphasizes ihtiyat (precaution) when blood stops early, because return is common. This affects not only intercourse but also the status of 'idda (waiting period) and ruj'a (right of return).
Al-Jundi and al-Nihayah further clarify the obligations and recommendations for ghusl timing.
5
Evaluate the proof and its strength
The proof is qiyas based on the nature of menstrual blood: it may flow intermittently. Ghusl or time passage establishes the side of stoppage. This is a zanni proof but is relied upon due to consistent practice.
Hanafi method uses istihsan (juristic preference) for precaution here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وإذا انقطع دم الحائض لأقل من عشرة أيام : لم يجز وطؤها حتى تغتسل، أو يمضي عليها وقت صلاة كاملة))؛ لأن الدم يدر تارةً، وينقطع تارةً، فلا بد من الاغتسال؛ ليترجح جانب الانقطاع. وقوله : كاملة : يُحترز عما إذا انقطع في وقت صلاة ناقصة، كصلاة الضحى والعيد، فإنه لا يجوز الوطء حتى تغتسل، أو يمضي وقت صلاة الظهر. وهذا إذا كان الانقطاع لعادتها، أما إذا كان لدونها: فإنه لا يجوز وطؤها وإن اغتسلت حتى تمضي عادتها؛ لأن العود في العادة غالب، فكان الاحتياط في الاجتناب. كذا في «الهداية». وفي «الجندي»: إذا انقطع دون عادتها : فإنها تغتسل وتصلي وتصوم ولا يطؤها زوجها حتى تمضي عادتها ؛ احتياطاً. ولو كان هذا في آخر حيضة من عدتها : بطلت الرجعة، وليس لها أن تتزوج غيره حتى تمضي عادتها، فيُؤخذ لها في ذلك كله بالاحتياط. وفي النهاية: إذا كانت عادتها دون العشرة، وانقطع الدم على العادة أخرت الغُسلَ إلى الوقت وتأخيره هنا استحباب، لا إيجاب. وإن كان الانقطاع دون العدة فتأخير الغسل إلى الوقت إيجاب.
His statement: (And if the blood of the menstruating woman stops for less than ten days: it is not permissible to have intercourse with her until she performs ghusl, or a complete prayer time passes over her) because blood flows sometimes and stops sometimes, so ghusl is necessary to establish the side of stoppage. And his statement: complete: is a precaution for if it stops during the time of an incomplete prayer, like the prayer of Duha and Eid, then intercourse is not permissible until she performs ghusl or the time of Dhuhr prayer passes. And this is if the stoppage is according to her habit. But if it is less than her habit: then it is not permissible to have intercourse with her even if she performs ghusl until her habit passes, because return to habit is common, so precaution is in avoidance. As in al-Hidayah. And in al-Jundi: if it stops less than her habit: she performs ghusl, prays, fasts, but her husband does not have intercourse with her until her habit passes, as a precaution. And if this is in the last menses of her waiting period: the right of return (ruj'a) is nullified, and she cannot marry another until her habit passes, so precaution is taken for her in all that. And in al-Nihayah: if her habit is less than ten days, and blood stops according to habit, she delays ghusl until the time, and its delay here is recommended, not obligatory. But if the stoppage is less than the habit, then delaying ghusl until the time is obligatory.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage from Sharh al-Jawhara explains the ruling from al-Quduri's matn on khiyār. The rule: if a woman's menstruation stops before ten days (the maximum duration), intercourse is not permissible until she performs ghusl or a complete prayer time passes. The reasoning is that blood may appear intermittently, so ghusl confirms the stoppage. The phrase 'complete' prayer time excludes incomplete prayers like Duha or Eid; the complete prayer time meant is that of Dhuhr (since it's fully encompassing). The passage then divides two scenarios: (1) if blood stops at her usual habit ('āda), the ruling applies as stated; (2) if it stops before her habit (i.e., earlier than usual), she must still wait until her whole habit passes, even if she performs ghusl, because it is likely to return. This is a precautionary measure (ihṭiyāṭ). It also discusses consequences: if this is the last menses of her 'idda, her right to return (ruj'a) is nullified, and she cannot remarry until the habit passes. Finally, it cites al-Nihayah on the ruling of delaying ghusl: if blood stops at the habit, delaying ghusl is recommended (mustaḥabb); if it stops before the habit, delaying is obligatory (wājib) until the habit passes.

Key Terms
حيض (hayḍ): Menstrual blood that occurs for a minimum of 3 days and maximum of 10 days
عادة (ʿāda): The woman's habitual number of menstruation days
وقت صلاة كامل (waqt ṣalāh kāmil): A complete prayer time, excluding times when only supererogatory prayers (like Duha) are offered
احتياط (iḥtiyāṭ): Precautionary measure to avoid violating the prohibition

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A woman's habitual menstruation is 6 days. Her blood stops on day 4. She performs ghusl. When is intercourse permissible according to the Hanafi school?
A) Immediately after ghusl
B) After a complete prayer time (e.g., Dhuhr) passes
C) After day 6 completes (her habit)
D) After 10 full days pass

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, after menstruation ends, intercourse is only permissible after ghusl or a complete prayer time; if blood stops before the woman's habitual duration, she must wait until the habit completes, even if she performs ghusl.
